		<username>Luuk-2</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>What was this actually all about?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What can I say, except that I looked forward to seeing it after having heard it praised on numerous occasions, and was severely disappointed. This film just would not take off, however hard it tried. Perhaps it is just as well, one dreads to think where it might have been off to if it had gotten off the ground. One reviewer described Louis Begley's book, on which the screenplay is based, as an ?intelligent, sympathetic portrait of an increasingly self-aware man in pursuit of unvarnished emotional truth'. If that is what the director aimed for, it was lost on me. Would an intelligent man ?celebrate' his new-found freedom from a nagging wife by peeing all over the bathroom? Would an intelligent assistant vice-president of a large insurance company buy so much fast food that his oversized camper bursts at the seams? Would a former company executive grab the first woman who shows some sympathy for his plight and force a kiss on her? Let me turn this around, why does a director feel he has to resort to such cliches as a man neglecting his house and himself just to show us he has become unhinged by his retirement, the death of his wife and the realization that no one (except little Ngudu) needs him any more, not even the daughter he has neglected for so many years. Do we really need more than two hours of preparation for the ?unvarnished emotional truth' in the form of one of the schmaltziest endings I have seen in recent years? Why didn't the director aim a little higher? Why didn't he try to equal (or better) Bergman's Wild Strawberries? What a shame about all the missed opportunities.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Here's a real wonder, and a nice choice by Jack Nicholson. Middle-aged VP suddenly becomes a widower and decides to find a purpose for his existence. It's a road movie, a good one, but--as per the genre--it doesn't really have an important agenda (and may feel rambling to some). Nicely attuned, well-directed piece with small, throwaway bits of comedy that actually convey quite a deal of truth. I felt the movie went to extremes for the sake of a cheap laugh only twice (in the trailer park scene with the neighbors and with Kathy Bates in the hot-tub), but Nicholson's purposefully low-wattage performance is wonderful, and much more complex than at first may seem. Jack is still a little young to be cast as a retired insuranceman in his waning years, but he brings Schmidt to life--he's a three-dimensional man in a one-dimensional world. Refreshing to see movies like this get made in the 21st Century. *** from ****</fullReview>

		<username>rsyung</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not great, but that's okay</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The thing I admire most about `About Schmidt' (or should I say about `Schmidt'?), is the fact that Jack Nicholson is a character you must submerge yourself in as an audience. If you don't, the movie is `boring' or `dull' in the words of other viewers. This is a risk most Hollywood Filmmakers are not willing to take anymore. Jack isn't `bigger than life', or `crazy like a Fox'. His character doesn't grab you by the throat and say `Watch me.' I'm a long way off from Warren's point in life, but there were times when I felt his discontent with his life, his love, and his rapidly diminishing future. Here was a man of average intelligence searching for a way out of a rapidly encroaching darkness and I felt that made him all the more poignant, but certainly not unlikeable. I guess that makes this a good movie, rather than a really great one. Great ones manage to transcend the differences we bring to the theater as an audience, allowing messages and themes to cross all manner of life experiences. Perhaps when a film succeeds in moving only a segment of that audience, it doesn't quite make it into the pantheon of `great' films, or at least one definition of them. So be it. The world needs more `good' films like this.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the most extraordinary films of the year</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alexander Payne's "About Schmidt" is a beautifully acted, marvelously photographed story of a 66-year old Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson), vice president of Woodman of the World, an insurance center in Omaha, Nebraska. After he retires, he faces another crossroad as his wife of many years dies. Then, with only his RV, he decides to rediscover himself along the way to his daughter's wedding. "About Schmidt" is filled with moments of sadness, irony, and the hardships of everyday life in America. "About Schmidt", although mainly a drama, has moments of laughter too. Many funny moments come from Schmidt's repressed life style when put to the test in contemporary society. Alexander Payne (who previously directed another extraordinary film, "Election") has a perfect way of telling a story that's sentimental, but not sappy, funny and sad, and real, but still worth watching. **** out of ****.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-04-28</date>
		<username>Jesse Allen (fidbox)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>About Payne!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Simply put, this film is a perfectly crafted mixture of Tragedy and Comedy. Both forces pull with equal intensity, and the final emotional result is something altogether new and beautiful. The film depicts and comments on overdue mid-life crises, aging, the concept of family, Midwest life/landscape, pilgrimages, regret, and acceptance (to name a few). But the tone of the film is not grounded in any way. `About Schmidt' lets viewer both laugh and cry at the screen - and this is what makes it so uniquely amazing.One element of the film's beauty, of course, is Jack Nicholson's performance. Jack IS Schmidt, down to the core. And because we all know Nicholson's on and off screen persona so well, he dazzles us all the more. But to put the focus solely on Nicholson's performance, and furthermore to dismiss the film as simply a great opportunity to see Nicholson at his best, would undermine beauty of the film.`About Schmidt' is a film with a great actor who was molded by an equally great director - Alexander Payne. For the most part, Payne has crafted each scene, as well as the overall plot structure, in a slow-paced and reserved manner. But it is this cinematic pacing that makes the film so extraordinarily funny and (at the same time) depressing. What Payne has created, with the HELP of Nicholson, is a film with a distinct `feeling.' If you're in the mood for a specific kind of film - one that is either dramatic or comedic, then this is not the film to watch. `About Schmidt' is simply the superb storytelling of certain kind of tragically humorous life/emotion - and it is beautiful.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>You want meaning? Try the dictionary.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a well?made, well-observed and very melancholy film about Warren, a very ordinary man who has lived his life in well-deserved obscurity in Omaha, Nebraska. The fact that he is played by Jack Nicholson means we go through the film waiting for him to explode, yet, in the end, he doesn't. After 42 years as an actuary (an accountant with a charisma by-pass) Warren slumps into retirement only to have his wife drop dead on him. She had made all the retirement plans, starting with a cross-country tour in a luxury Winnebago mobile home, and he is at a loose end. Wither Warren? He goes to his daughter's wedding in Denver, Colorado, and there's a bit of a story in that, but essentially Warren is bereft. Jack Nicholson's performance as Warren reminded me of his grumpy writer in `As Good as it Gets' but this time he is older, fatter and quieter. But not boring ? Jack is incapable of that. Instead we get a very internal movie ? despite the vast vistas of the mid-west what is put before us is the inner suffering of a man who realises his life is all but over and it has been as boring as bat sh*t. A lifetime of suppressing his feelings has made it difficult for him to express his love for his daughter (nicely played by Hope Davis), and now she is about to marry beneath her (a water-bed salesman). We know that Warren can feel ? his letters to the Tanzanian child he is sponsoring tell us that - but he long ago stopped acting on his feelings and succumbed to routine and his wife's tender care.Alexander Payne (`Election') is from Nebraska and through his direction gives us a sense of time and place, yet the story is a universal one. Perhaps the lesson is, at least if you are under 40, even if you can't get out of that nice safe job in the bank, develop some other real interests, preferably involving real people. Even Warren was a bright young thing once, as we see in his old College photos. (I shudder to think what Jack was like at college). Academy award? Jack's had enough, but Kathy Bates deserves one for stepping into the hot tub with Jack.</fullReview>

The rest of the cast is variable; Kathy Bates runs away with the show, and Dermot Mulroney, unrecognizable under his sparse wig and mutton chops, brings dignity and likeability to the character Schmidt despises so much. But Hope Davis barely registers as a presence in her pivotal role, and when she does she comes off as impatient and ugly.As for the movie itself, it's a mixed bag. There's genuine emotion at its heart, and the title character is a complex creation. But it's also meandering in structure (a set-piece in a trailer park is a complete misfire), and the script is curiously flat much of the time. It's sad, but not terribly; and it's funny too, but not terribly. I think too much has been made of Alexander Payne's misanthropy, here and elsewhere; that said, the set decorator must have gone blind searching out the most hideous wallpapers and upholsteries to define these characters. But the movie certainly wants to do more than mock Midwesterners. It's just hard to tell what, exactly. 6 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2003-02-15</date>
		<username>R. J. (jorge.mourinha@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Bleak, dispassionate tragedy with a career-defining performance by Jack Nicholson</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a follow-up to the critically acclaimed "Election" and to his scripting of "Jurassic Park III", you could hardly expect a more different movie from writer-director Alexander Payne. Loosely based on Louis Begley's novel but mostly borrowing from an earlier, unproduced script by Payne and his co-writer Jim Taylor, "About Schmidt" is a tragically, relentlessly bleak and desperate portrait of a man at a crossroads from where he's got nowhere to go: newly retired and newly widowed insurance actuary Warren R. Schmidt, whose only links to a life spent working to keep the family afloat are a dysfunctional relationship with a daughter living away who is about to marry a man he doesn't like and a young African boy he sponsors through a childcare organisation to whom he bares his soul open in the letters he mails regularly. Many have billed the film as comedy or satire, but in fact "About Schmidt" is rather the modern-day tragedy of seeing your life waste away and not doing anything to counter it. It's the sort of film that requires a career-defining performance from its lead, and here Payne gives us the exquisite gift of Jack Nicholson at the peak of his art, effectively effacing his larger-than-life persona behind a subtly nuanced portrait of a man mourning his life. "About Schmidt" is neither easy-going nor perfect (you often wonder if it's real poignancy Payne is striving after or if he is being merely cruelly cynical or callous), but the simple fact that the doubt is there makes it one of the most intriguing American films in recent years.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2003-02-14</date>
		<username>rogerdarlington</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Let's have more such character-driven movies</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is certainly not your standard Hollywood fare. For a start, the central character is not a pubescent teenager or a comic strip super-hero, but a former insurance man become brutally aware of the fragility - and indeed the futility - of his life. Recently retired, even more recently widowed and awaiting his daughter's wedding to a man he believes to be a nincompoop, 66 year old Warren Schmidt of Omaha, Nebraska is - in the words of one woman he meets on the road with his huge trailer home - "a sad, sad man".As the eponymous loner, Jack Nicholson gives one of the finest performances of his long and distinguished career, a magnificently understated portrayal in which a look, a grimace or a tear conveys so much about his inner torment and deep melancholy. He is supported by a series of finely-observed vignettes, none better than that from Kathy Bates who bravely reveals her less than svelte-like body. There is no simple resolution to Schmidt's dilemma, but he is ultimately given an insight into how even his selfish life has made a difference. Full of pathos and wry humour, great credit then to Louis Begley who authored the original novel and to Alexander Payne who co-wrote the script and directed. We need more character-driven movies like this which reflect life as most of us find it - frequently disappointing but never too late to redeem.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>yaseminturkish</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>My vote remains with Alexander Payne's 1999 film "Election"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"About Schmidt" could have been so much more but instead follows the course of "American Beauty" as one of the most overrated films in recent history. Alexander Payne created a masterpiece with "Election" in 1999, and so I had high hopes that his direction and Jack Nicholson's talent could propel this film to Oscar glory. Instead, the tone was too subtle and the Warren Schmidt character too unlikeable for me to care much at all.Sure the sponsorship of little orphan Ndugu was touching along with the memorable ending that saved the film, but was it enough? Leaving the theater, I had the feeling that more could have been done with the waterbed salesman son-in-law and the short-lived scenes with Kathy Bates as the hippie-esque Roberta. The relationship with Schimdt's daughter could have been fleshed out a little. Mostly, I wanted to exit the theater feeling sad for this forgotten old man who you'd never miss if he vanished off the face of the Earth. Instead, I just left with thoughts of Ndugu, the orphan we never even see on-screen but whose missing presence presented the only emotion in the film.Jack Nicholson did what he could but not even his miserable existence could make me like Warren Schmidt. I secretly wished that it had been Warren and not his wife that had passed on, perhaps she would have been a more exciting character. Watching paint dry or reading all 6,000 pages of War and Peace would be more entertaining then watching Warren make sense of his final years.Also perplexing is the subtlety of the film which dragged everything out, such a departure from the all-out satire of "Election." Some critics denounced Payne's lack of subtlety with "Election" but too much subtlety is also a bad thing as is evidenced with "About Schimdt." There were some nice moments but this is by no means the best film of the year. Make plans to see "Far From Heaven" if your looking for that.</fullReview>

		<username>Chris_Docker</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Nicholson's comedic approach to later years</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I sometimes indulge in wondering, as I'm sure most people do, if my life has any fundamental meaning or value, whether or not I have ever made a real difference, etc etc and other such quasi-philosophical ramblings. It is the story of such internal soliloquy that makes up the substance ? padded out by Jack Nicholson for almost every frame of this not insubstantial movie - of what could have been a remarkable tale of self-examination and self-realisation. Playing a wittery old man certainly might be a timely challenge for Nicholson, but the question remains, in spite of plaudits for his performance, of whether he is really up to it.(Contains some plot spoilers): Schmidt (Nicholson) retires honourably as vice president of an insurance firm. The bleak, wintry cityscape of high rise buildings that opens the movie, a sort of respectable but unenviably mediocre world that any right-minded child would aspire to rise above one day, is a perfect metaphor for the emptiness of the central character's existence. He finds his age and wisdom is politely discarded, that no-one really seems to care what he thinks, and life just goes on from day to day as he tolerates his wife ? `an elderly woman living in his house'. When his wife dies suddenly, that is the first of his wake up calls as to the pointlessness of his own life. After deciding, retrospectively, that he loves her, he discovers she was unfaithful to him. A series of incidents confirms that he is ?surplus to requirements', except perhaps to write cheques for his daughter's wedding to a man of whom he disapproves intensely. The final sugar-sweet ending is predictable, even if delivered with a certain flourish, and aspires to be far more deep and meaningful than it is. Proceedings are only momentarily enlivened by the flash of (54yr old) Kathy Bates' pendulous bosoms when, as the twice-divorced and highly-sexed mother of the prospective son-in-law, she romps naked into a hot-tub to flirt with the flabbergasted Schmidt.The film could perhaps have been more successful if a talented unknown had played the lead. We are too used to Nicholson playing Nicholson ? either mad, bad, or heavily tongue in cheek. This reviewer felt he was a little too large for the part, although many, especially American, audiences may find that the small restraint he at least manages to show somehow marks his performance as ?understated.' It was still a good movie ? I was just rather underwhelmed.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-01-28</date>
		<username>Alexander Sultoon</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Just About Schmidt</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>About Schmidt...and not much else, being both the strength and weakness of this film. Jack Nicholson stars as the newly retired Warren Schmidt, facing his retirement with the knowledge that he has a 'seventy three percent chance of dying in the next nine years', gleaned from his boring job as an actuary. Desperate to seek a sense of worth that has been missing from his monotonous existence, he sponsors a Tanzanian child and spills his heart in series of rambling letters. Unforeseen events then force him to re-evaluate the purpose of his life and he sets out to dissuade his only daughter from marrying a nerdy waterbed salesman, who threatens the high hopes he had for her.Nicholson delivers an engaging performance as the sad and lonely Schmidt and there are plenty of laughs to be had, though mostly about ageing and the bodily functions thereof. Unfortunately, Nicholson lacks a strong supporting character to play off, leaving him dramatically isolated which he shouldn't be, rather than emotionally isolated, which he should be. Having started promisingly, the plot seems to fizzle out halfway through, leaving a great deal of dramatic potential unexplored for the sake of of lugubrious meanderings. Furthermore, the emotional climax at the end connected on an intellectual level, but failed to wrench the audience as it needed if it were to be a truly moving and poignant film.Nevertheless About Schmidt is a well-crafted piece with some fine acting and many entertaining moments, but it needed to be about someone else too.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Who gives a Schmidt?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm afraid I'm getting rather tired of this new style of movie making where we forgo plot and character for director and actor. It doesn't matter that the story is so full of inconsistencies it had me giving my head a shake. Once you have achieved star status as an actor or director then your work becomes about you, rather than the story you are telling your audience. This movie is about Jack Nicholson the way Signs is about M. Night Shyamalan. So what ends up happening is the majority of movie goers (at least most of the people in attendance at the theatre I was in) already like the movie before they have seen it. We were told "wait until you see Jack Nicholson play someone his own age". The only scene that totally worked for me, where I didn't see "Jack Nicholson acting his age" but actually felt something for the man who lost his wife and realized he missed the comfort of routine and comfort of his wife's presence, was when he was putting her face cream on his face in heartbreaking urgency. Oddly enough however, the audience found this hysterical. "Oh, look. Jack's doing something funny."The director used the usual cliches about a man who retires - return to the office and find you're not needed. Really? Gee, I thought they'd give him another job; the man who loses his wife and is suddenly on his own - messy kitchen, clothes everywhere as if that's a given when you're suddenly on your own; as if there is no man of any intelligence who hasn't got the ability to clean up or pick up after himself. The argument of course is that he's gone into a mild depression but as a director, why not stretch yourself and come up with a different way, maybe even a symbolic way, to show us Schmidt's inner self. Messy kitchen and a pile of dirty clothes ain't art.Kathy Bates, one of the finest actors ever to grace the screen, was also given a paint-by-numbers character and handed the usual "schock schlock stock" dialogue to go with it - orgasms, breast feeding, cursing and nudity. Puh-leeze.About Schmidt is about Jack and Kathy. That's fine if that's all you want. If you want more, then watch "The Last Detail" or "As Good As It Gets" for not-just-Jack and "Misery", "Dolores Claiborne" or "Fried Green Tomatoes" for not-just-Kathy.</fullReview>

You don't need some huge burst of music to get the sense that this man is lost, even while struggling. The family relations are so much more real to me than the shrill performances of Annette Bening and Kevin Spacey: whereas that movie FELT scripted, this feels very organic. Again, the majority of what needs to be said never gets there. All we have to really know this guy is through his letters to a 6-yr. old African child, and these moments are really the most poignant in the film. I CONNECTED with Schmidt, and what's most telling is that HE couldn't connect with anyone, much perhaps like Ben Braddock back all those years ago. Her part could have been played down, but because of the powerhouse of an actor she is, she took the audience on a wild ride, from cursing to boobies to bad hats. And not once did you take your eyes off of her.Nicholson and Bates deserve every award they get for "About Schmidt", but the rest of the team behind the film needs to rethink a few things before putting out a film that goes nowehere.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-01-05</date>
		<username>movieguy1021 (Movieguy1021@comcast.net)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>About Schmidt: 7/10</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>We meet Warren R. Schmidt as he's sitting in his office, briefcase in hand, staring at the clock. When 5:00 finally comes, he leaves and shuts off his light. He is now officially a retired man. He's not exactly the happiest man in the world: he and his wife aren't exactly best of friends, he got taken over by someone forty years younger than he, and he's not super-friendly with his daughter Jeannie (Hope Davis). And he's not happy about her choice of husband: Randall Hertzel (Dermot Mulroney), a mullet-wearing `dude' who works in a waterbed store.Schmidt's wife Helen (June Squibb) suddenly dies, and he realizes how much he took for granted. He wants to do something with his life, so he sponsors a child in Africa named Udugu and goes on a road trip in a Winnebago to ruin his daughter's wedding.Jack Nicholson, who plays Schmidt, is perfectly cast (against his earlier work). He's somewhat of a cynic and his facial expressions (that don't change, just like a certain Mr. Clint Eastwood) sometimes even seemed sinister, even though his character didn't support that. However, Jack Nicholson can't stop being Jack Nicholson. I expected him to come out and say, `And what do you expect me to do about it?' I'm not saying he wasn't good. His performance was extremely good, and he showed emotion, though not physically. I could just tell that he was giving his all into the role.However, I didn't feel very emotionally attached to the main character. Sure, I felt with him (like all good movies should do), but I didn't feel like I was going along for a ride with him. I could, however, tell that director Alexander Payne put a lot of symbolism, such as Schmidt liked driving his Winnebago because he was looking down on people, making him feel important. However, when the horses came, it made him feel caged up again.The pace was slow but sure. New Line, who released the film, knew that the majority of people who they appeal to (they did the Austin Powers and Lord of the Rings series) would not like it, so they could do whatever they wanted in it. So they deliberately paced is slowly, but it didn't drag down the proceedings. In some ways, this movie reminded me of Ghost World, because Schmidt is slowly seeing that his world he loved is falling to shambles. He went to visit his home where he lived before, but, to keep the secret and surprise, it wasn't there. He couldn't bear to, after his wife's passing, see more of his life go away, so he has to keep his daughter.About Schmidt was in two parts: one of him coping with the loss and the second of going to his daughter's wedding. The music was gentle but brought out the mood. Sometimes the score was absent, improving the scene more. The opening camera angles were very impressive; I liked them a lot. It seemed like how the scenes were cut were sharp and quick, but worked well with the subject matter.Possibly the best, and worst, part of the film is Kathy Bates. She's Randall's mother, and she is quite good. However, as you've probably heard by now, she has a nude scene. Brrr? Thankfully, it's brief. She had outrageous costumes which added to her (barely developed) character.The deeper meaning of About Schmidt is to do the best you can in life. Schmidt didn't, and he felt unused and insignificant. The end had something happen that really made him think about himself. You can look at your life in two ways: either you didn't do all you wanted to or you did everything you could. Schmidt did the former, but didn't even think about the latter. The ending, by the way, is quite nice.The movie is played as a drama, but it had its funny moments. Most were about Schmidt's quirks, but a few were sight gags and slapstick. It was used, successfully, to lighten the mood. So, About Schmidt is a touching, if emotionally lacking, film about life.My rating: 7/10Rated R for some language and brief nudity.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-12-26</date>
		<username>Zen Bones</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Jack grows up</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Although it would seem to be a major disgrace to cast the hippest and most eccentric geriatric in cinema to portray a man of such blandness and inconsequence, it comes off as inspired casting here. This is a part that cries out against type. Had someone undistinguished played the part, such as a modern day Wally Cox or say, Tom Hanks in twenty years, the edge that is Schmidt's general malaise, and the contempt he feels for his failings in life would not have been profoundly prevalent in this film. And they should be. This is thankfully not "Forrest Gump 2" or any of the other usual Hollywood fare with the glossed over 'life is a box of chocolates" philosophy. Yes, this film is about the acceptance that being a nice person is not a sign of being bland, but a sign of having a great deal of character and value in the world. But it is also about the unsettling edge and sense of denial that lives in each of us, and the desperate need to be important to somebody or some thing. Nicholson thankfully does not ham things up with his usual robust ticks of being simultaneously cocky and charming. There is a lot of anger in the character of Warren Schmidt, but Jack wisely doesn't show it. This character can't since he isn't even really aware of it. He takes his own feelings of mediocrity out on others, like his lackluster son-in-law and "the old lady sleeping in bed next to him" (his wife).I've heard that the book on which this film is based took place in Manhattan. It's hard for me to imagine what this film would have been like in such a setting. Alexander Payne, who is a native of Omaha, beautifully captures the generic banality of Middle America. If Schmidt lived in a "hip" city like Manhattan, he would be coiled up in venom. It is important that he is so ensconced in his generic surroundings, that he can't recognize the depths of his angst. Setting Warren Schmidt in Manhattan would sort of be like FIVE EASY PIECES thirty years later. He'd be a man who had run away from the exceptional qualities within him, and was now full of regrets. We've seen that sort of story before. Perhaps this film is a "what if" FIVE EASY PIECES, with Jack as a man who never knew he had any exceptional qualities to begin with. Spending one's life in a place where the sense of something sacred or beautiful is reduced to church socials and little figurines that one buys on the Home Shopping Network, will very likely create a malaise of some sort eventually. To rebel against that in an old-aged rant of fury would be pure Hollywood: the familiar Jack the Rebel. While it is the role of youth to rant against the mediocrity that stagnates our society and to take a stand against it, it is the mark of maturity to grow from being against everything to being about something. One only needs to open their eyes and look deep within oneself and around oneself in order to find out what they are about. Our daily lives and our cities may not be exceptional, but we as human beings can be. If we can just forgive the flaws within ourselves, the rest is easy.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"About" perfect; "Schmidt" happens to Jack! The Best Film of 2002</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>ABOUT SCHMIDT (2002) **** Jack Nicholson, Hope Davis, Dermot Mulroney, Kathy Bates, June Squibb, Howard Hesseman, Len Cariou. Nicholson gives a career high performance as Warren Schmidt, a recently retired life insurance actuary from Omaha, Nebraska who comes to an emotional crossroads in his life when his wife suddenly dies and his only daughter is about to marry a complete moron. Schmidt seeks some meaning for his existence and goes on a literal and metaphorical road trip, Winnebago at the helm, across America as he ventures to the wedding ceremony accruing while realizing that life will always take care of itself even if one doesn't know what will become of themselves (no truer thought has ever been realized quite comically nor clearly as it does here). Filmmaker Alexander Payne (who adapted Louis Begley's novel with his longtime collaborator Jim Taylor) shrewdly mixes the mundane with the meaningful in an acrid slice of Americana that threatens to curdle as it nurtures along its merrily demented way. Nicholson smartly downplays the easily caricatured character it could've been by ditching his waycool persona and physically resembles a shrugged shoulder replete with bad combover and easily to his 12th Academy Award nomination and possibly 4th win. Immersing himself wholeheartedly and - who expected, quite poignantly - brilliantly; one of the finest interpretations ever displayed in cinematic history (and that's saying something for this icon's canon of film work!) Bates is a riot as his in-law to be who has no qualms about herself - a complete 180 from Nicholson's impression - and Mulroney lets the mullet do all the work smartly. Davis manages the tightrope of good daughter and angry child within allowing her character's seams to show at her worst and deep down loves her estranged father. So far the year's best film and one I can humbly add has my surname in the title!</fullReview>
	</review>

If it isn't for Jack Nicholson's awesome acting (definitely Oscar worthy performance!) and the obviously and astonishingly distasteful draw on human flaws and sociopolitical boldness to humor the moviegoers, I'd say, it's one tale that's hard to swallow and unemotionally dull to watch. 		<shortDescription>View of Hope in the Face of Defeat</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What I really enjoy about this film is that the overall film produces a large feeling of hopelessness within you -- a hopelessness that is very real, very true, very genuine, and such a very human experience. But what this film does is not simply crush and destroy your hope for the future, but it demonstrates that in the face of hopelessness there is still... Hope? Or should I say -- moments of pleasure, moments of joy, and small, little events that make life worth living. The film, from the beginning to the end, shows such a great unraveling of a life, such a great shedding of the skin -- you begin to understand what it means to be a human being (and not a fantastical character, but rather just an ordinary human). It is a borderline spiritual event: with 'About Schmidt' we all realize the hopelessness of life, and shed the old skin that we had worn, and realize that our hopes and our dreams should lie in a new direction. We also learn that we should step back and analyze the world for what it is, as we see that Schmidt cannot control his life the way that we wanted to, and as we see that Schmidt is overlooked, we learn a lesson: happiness will always have to come from within, and our hopes can never be unrealistic.A great film with a great lesson in it -- brutally honest. If you enjoyed this film, or would like to see more like it, I would recommend Akira Kurosawa's Ikiru.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Deeply flawed but at least makes you think</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I didn't particularly enjoy this movie because I wanted it to get up and go but it merely went away. I take it as a depressing, cynical, condescending look at a retired insurance actuary's life in retirement. It was an indictment of midwestern life, its people, and the way they act. This aspect was done much better in "Fargo". In the first place, Schmidt was a simpleton, a dolt who couldn't comb his own hair - not particularly accurate for the actuaries I've known who are generally brilliant people. The gloomy panning of the Omaha skyline is a not too subtle cheap shot at the midwest, as is the entire movie and its characters. The in-law family and the wedding scene were actually quite accurate in depicting the goofiness some people and families display at functions in everyday life. It wasn't particularly interesting to watch such people and I wonder if the makers of this film understand that we see movies to be entertained, not bored by the underbelly of society. This movie wants to say that Schmidt is a hopeless dolt who cannot handle his retirement and his life and whose emotions are so bottled up that it looks like he's ready to explode at any minute. Is this enjoyable, instructive, meaningful, entertaining? I think of it as just another pretentious attempt to create a classic of filmdom while managing to bore the Hell out of the majority of us viewers. Consider the bonus material on the DVD. There are long strings of text as preambles to the outtakes that we have to wade through before viewing them. A simple suggestion to the director - how about you telling us about each outtake with your voice instead of forcing us to read the drivel that precedes them? Yes, we're forced to read that material and we're forced to think about Schmidt's dilemma, not all bad, but it is so utterly depressing in the process that on balance we are not stimulated or entertained and if we don't have either of those we are not pleased with the experience. Giving it a rating of 6 is very generous.</fullReview>

		<username>bob the moo</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Nicholson carries it from start to finish and makes it significantly better than it could have been</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Warren Schmidt is in his mid-sixties and has recently retired from his long service employment with one of the leading insurance companies in Nebraska. On retirement though he finds himself with little to do and unsure of how he now fits into the world. His wife is little more than an old woman that lives with him, his job no longer seems to have had any purpose and the distance he feels from his family is intense. When his wife suddenly passes away, Schmidt finds himself at a loose end before deciding to head to see his daughter in his new Winnebago.The plot summary does make it sound like a rather obvious affair and I suppose in a way that is just what it is. The film looks at old age through the rather aimless eyes of Warren Schmidt and in doing so produces a funny and touching story that doesn't really force a "message" down the viewers throat even if that does produce the feeling that it didn't go anywhere. The narrative is rather rambling but it held my attention because I found each section interesting or comic and generally it all held together. The script could have been stronger though because I felt it was weakened by the way it tended to look down on all its characters rather than love and embrace them despite their faults. This did take away from how much of a character piece the film could be but, fortunately, it plays its one big trump card from start to finish.This card is a Jack Nicholson performance that surprised me throughout. I have so used to seeing him living his playboy personae in films and the Oscars that to actually catch him acting was a pleasant surprise. He delivers a contained and restrained performance that holds the entire story together because so much of it is played out in his face and body language. As a result the support cast is very much just that. Bates is OK, Davis is a nice find while Mulroney is well cast and funny and Hesseman is good in a tiny role. However Payne knows where his strength lies and the film focuses on Nicholson as a result.Overall then a comic and touching wander through old age. It isn't perfect by any means but luckily a wonderful performance from Jack Nicholson covers over the cracks and makes the whole thing better than it could have been.</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2004-11-05</date>
		<username>CurtMan@LVCM.com</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Curious Character Study Propelled By Brilliant Nicholson Performance</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"About Schmidt" is about as humble, low-key, and unpretentious as films come, and mostly succeeds strictly as a character study of confused, reflective senior living, in the form of the ordinary Warren Schmidt, played with brilliance and compassion by the always great Jack Nicholson. The entire premise involves the goings and reflections of Schmidt, in a narrative where he is frequently addressing letters of wisdom and candidness to a naive six year old Tanzanian boy, disclosing practically every detail of his recent life. There's no way around it, if you are not interested in learning about the life of a common, tamed man (ironically played by Jack Nicholson, as eccentric and zany as they come), then there is absolutely nothing you can appreciate from this film, as it is strictly an exploration of "the values of life discovered much too late" and there are hardly any major developments or surprises to be found here. While it works harmoniously in this department, from someone who enjoys a good character study, I found the premise and the dialogue to be too simple, too practical, and missing a much needed conflict (unless you can argue that Schmidt's "self discovery" is a conflict of redemption for all of his many wasted years and everything he has- including his defiant and unappreciative daughter). Nicholson, as usual, gets completely lost in this character, and he is always very absorbing and convincing, even playing perhaps the antithesis of his casual "wild" role. The story is engaging enough, with Schmidt, after suddenly losing his life and retiring from his lowly job, searching for all of the values and experiences in life he has neglected for the past forty years, in reflecting and revisiting the happiness he once possessed early on in life, yet due to many circumstances, has completely forgotten about these values of his existence. In addition, he tries to rekindle his estranged relationship with his daughter, Jeannie, and perhaps even prevent her from marrying a man who is, well, not "up to snub" for her, as he incites. The scenery of the countryside of the midwest is gorgeous, breathtaking, and perfectly appropriate for this theme of solitude and rediscovery which defines this film. "About Schmidt"'s few detractions come from its rather dull screenplay, queer dialogue, and wandering tone: a blend of such serious drama and such raunchy comedy (e.g. the hot tub scene between Nicholson and Bates, the motorhome mishap, etc.) appears very odd in such a film as this. However, the engaging, delightful, and compassionate performances of Jack Nicholson and Kathy Bates, as well as some absorbing music, breathtaking scenery, and engaging plot, make "About Schmidt" something about worth seeing, particularly if you enjoy a vivid character study. *** out of ****</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>About Schmidt is at best an opportunity lost and a disappointment on at last two counts.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILER-----SPOILER------SPOILERAbout Schmidt is a serious drama about life's big events; marriage, retirement, death and social contribution. Warren Schmidt [Jack Nicholson] has turned 65 years old and just retired; he immediately realizes [surprise, surprise] that he left his life at the office and has not a clue of what comes next. Tragedy soon strikes the Schmidt home and Warren realizes that he's got to get a life. He initially sees his daughter Jeannie [Hope Davis] as the solution, but that works only for 1-2 meals. Warren decides to jump in the RV and meanders around Nebraska and Kansas looking for the meaning of life. After what seems like an eternity [yet only 125 minutes later] he arrives in Denver for Jeannie' wedding where he meets her future in-laws. Enter the 2002 version of TV's Adams Family led by matriarch Roberta [Kathy Bates]. Warren barely survives the wedding and, upon returning to Omaha, finds his calling in a letter from Africa. `Fini'. About Schmidt is at best an opportunity lost and a disappointment on at last two counts. First, in an effort to attract the mass market, the previews cherry pick several humorous scenes to represent the movie as a comedy, nothing could be further from the truth. Second, and far more serious, the real themes of the movie exceed the technical reach of filmmakers. Any good film uses the cinematic tools of script, dialogue, scenes, sets, plot, story line and action [motion] to tell a story. Unable to use these tools, the message is conveyed with a voice-over narration by Schmidt himself; this runs over about 40-50% of the film. Sounds to me more like ?books on tape' than a movie. The story line---such as it is---raises issues of substance, but these are ill developed and don't really come to closure in any meaningful way. About Schmidt does have one bright spot after a dreary first 60+ minutes. Kathy Bates joins the story as the mother of the groom and an aging hippie somewhere between husbands two and three, but who's counting. She adds a desperately needed spark of life to the film and bares all for a lost cause, in the quintessential hot tub scene.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Not About Schmidt</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I remember reading, when this film was released, that the writer on whose novel it was based, Louis Begley, thought the screen adaptation was a good job.Either Mr. Begley had been paid extraordinarily well for the rights to his book, or he was just lying.It's not that this is a bad film. Not at all! It's quite well-done. But any resemblance between this film and the novel on which it was based is coincidental.Mr. Begley really ought to be permitted to sell screen rights to his book a second time -- the story he penned has never been made into a movie! The tone, the social class of the characters depicted, the relationships among the characters -- all are vastly different in book and film.To me, the best adaptations are those that the viewer finds recognizable -- familiar -- rewarding because of some proximity between one's separate experiences as reader and as film-goer.But "About Schmidt" was not about Schmidt. It was about some other guy. An interesting guy; funny guy. A guy that poignant things happened to. But not Schmidt.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>About Schmidt starts out promisingly but quickly loses focus from the issues it establishes early in the film - specifically, Nicholson dealing with retirement and the sudden death of his wife. 		<fullReview>I knew I was going to dislike this, as I have a real distaste for Alexander Payne's films, so I probably should have just skipped it. But I had a chance to see it for free, so I did. And ugh. It is such an ugly and cynical movie, with obvious themes that, if a better writer or director had handled them, wouldn't have seemed nearly so trite. It is in vogue for critics to claim that certain filmmakers revel in deriding their characters, and it's a criticism that I personally don't like to make, but Payne is one who spends most of the time looking down on the poor saps in his creations. All one has to do to prove that in About Schmidt is to point out Dermot Mulroney. Payne's idea of humor is to give him a mullet, and to make sure that the audience can always see the mullet. I'll bet the audience in the theater (and this is the type of film that attracts pseudo-intellectual, semi-yuppies) was cracking up at it. I watched it alone on a television, so all I had was my own screams of horror. And the letters to Ndugu were on a whole other terrible level, as the depth of the `joke' is more complex; these emotional testimonies should really be touching, but instead Payne constantly has to remind us that Schmidt's pains are as insignificant as Schmidt himself. This probably wasn't even Payne's intention, but he is such an inept writer that he shoots himself in the foot with his own poorly judged gag. Well, the one positive thing I can say about the film is that Jack Nicholson finds every shred of humanity in Warren Schmidt that he can. If it succeeds on any emotional level at all, it is because Nicholson is such a great actor. Kathy Bates, however, in a highly celebrated role, is embarrassing. She was Oscar nominated for getting naked. Otherwise, she is just another one-dimensional character for Payne to tease. 4/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Depressing, motionless</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I know many people will disagree with me, but this didn't seem so much like a movie as it did a photograph, or a 6-panel comic.I should explain that a bit. See, normally in a story, you expect the characters to go somewhere internally, to change in some way. This is what separates 2D characters from 3D characters.Unfortunately, that seemed to be missing from About Schmidt. In fact, the the main character (Schmidt) spent the entire movie trying to get people to change, without success. By the end of the movie, he has simply given up. The major internal change in Schmidt is that he has lowered his expectations, and now no longer expects life to be anything but bad.Of course, there is the part about "adopting" the child, which allows Schmidt to narrate his internal thoughts as the movie progresses. However because his internal exploration is fairly monotonous, the viewer (in this case, me) may finish the movie and be totally confused. Is the message that the only way to find some glint of happiness in life is by sending money to charity? Or that you can do your best, but the people who know you best will never appreciate you? I should note that this confusion was even greater upon viewing the VHS, which ends with an advertisement for the charity shown in the movie. Was the whole thing some kind of conspiracy to induce selflessness?In any case, this movie starts off in a grey mood, and ends in a grey mood. The writers tried to fill the movie with black comedy, but it only serves to make the entire affair more depressing.As for the realism of the characters, perhaps that's true. But the events conspire in such a ridiculously contrived way that it ruins the effect.I'm not usually against sad films, or really dark comedy. But this movie seems to be a one-off attempt at depressing the audience; the shock-flick of sadness, if you will.If you don't know where to look for depressing things in real life, and you want to feel bad, then this movie is for you. Otherwise, I wouldn't recommend it.IMHO, of course.</fullReview>

		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>a very generation-specific film...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>...A generation of which I'm not a part of (I'm 21). I saw this with my dad, and as we talked about it on the way back home, he was revealing to me all these tiny plot details that I had missed altogether (or couldn't comprehend), not that they amounted to a sensible, well-balanced movie in the end. Yes, after a backlog of coming-of-age films regarding teenagers ("The Breakfast Club," "subUrbia," "Ghost World"), we now have one for seniors, which does nothing but reinforce the tired mantra of life being meaningless."About Schmidt" is the kind of indigestible sappiness that critics and 'The Academy' drool over. When they refer to Jack Nicholson's painfully restrained (I don't care how relevant it was to the character, he was just DULL) performance, they don't refer to it just in relation to the film, but "his sprawling career," trying desperately to justify the 'daring' of his acting chops and the film itself. The supporting cast is a collection of one-dimensional oddities (they don't deserve the privilege of being called 'characters'). Director Alexander Payne, who helmed the hilarious "Election," obviously wanted to do something different, but this is a pathetic instance of a filmmaker going too far in the WRONG direction. "About Schmidt" is playing into the hands of critics for its entire running time (and, granted, it's the type of bleak, empty dramatic portrait that gains high praise for its supposed 'depth'). Not only is it filled with unfunny, unfeeling characters you could care less about, it contains lots of unforgivably cliched symbolism. For instance: Nicholson is camping out on top of his monstrous Winnebago one night, talking up to his deceased wife; when he finishes, a shooting star actually flashes by (followed by Nicholson 'crossing' himself)! Or what about the poor, South-American child he sponsors, leading up to the heavy-handed 'picture' scene at the very end of the movie? Give me a break."About Schmidt" just chugs along with nary a purpose--a remarkably empty film that, in the end, amounts to jack schmidt.3/10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Schmidt was full of it. Filler that is. It was the dullest film I've started watching in ten years.I'm not a Nicholson fan, &amp; Schmidt (that's a beer, right?) didn't help. And you *did*, Jack.As for the "point" of the "story", 1956's "The Man in the Grey Flannel Suit" developed this theme decades earlier. Joni Mitchell's song "The Arrangement" which sings about a man who "could have been more than a name on the door / on the 33rd floor in the air / More than a credit card, swimming pool in the backyard", makes the same "point" much more movingly in a song that lasts a few minutes. The suburban lifestyle *was* adequately pilloried in the 70s. Then, that 70s generation went on to develop it far beyond the vapid nothingness it represented in those happier, if blinkered, times. Nicholson was part of that generation. He did pretty well at cashing in.No doubt many people who don't like this film see themselves reflected in it just a litte *too* much. But stop and consider that Schmidt pretty much did all the things majoritarian American culture asked of him. Dressed right, lied convincingly, built a good home. He was even a part of the insurance industry, which a great majority of Americans invest heavily in. Is majoritarian American culture, then, life-avoiding ... or is it a facile if uninspiring adaptation to the world full of violence, duplicity and tedium Jarmusch paints in "Dead Man"? "Schmidt" is an easy exercise in mud-tossing. It's easy to criticize existing instutions without a vision of viable alternatives. The roles people have been offered and played faithfully in our culture may well be vapid, but what choices did they have? In failing to address how the options were engineered, "Schmidt" writes a check (for $22 to a foster child, about as magnanimous as the Thompson approach to welfare) that it, like so many films of the 70s era, can't cash.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2007-10-28</date>
		<username>DAVID SIM</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Jack Nicholson's finest film performance</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Warren R Schmidt has an epiphany only days after his retirement. He's completely wasted the last 66 years of his life.About Schmidt is one of the finest and truest satires I've seen in some time. It comes from director Alexander Payne who previously helmed the sharp and unnervingly accurate teen comedy Election. He brings the exact same intuition to About Schmidt that consistently fired up Election.The haunting portrait of a newly retired man unsure which direction to take at the crossroads of his life, Warren Schmidt is brought wonderfully to life by Jack Nicholson.I must confess I normally find Jack Nicholson a difficult actor to stomach. He turns in good performances when characters fit like a glove. Such as The Joker. Or the Devil in The Witches of Eastwick. But when the character's all wrong for him, the results are really quite tedious, like Jack Torrance in The Shining.The good news is Warren Schmidt is a character that suits him down to the ground. In fact this may be the best bit of acting Nicholson has ever done. Its certainly his most inhibited.The opening scene witnesses the end of Schmidt's job as Assistant Vice President of Woodman Insurance Co. He sits in his office, patiently waiting for the day to end by intently staring at the clock on the wall. His briefcase is already packed and ready to go. And as soon as it hits 5 o'clock, he trudges out the door.His retirement party is a parade of empty promises and half-hearted moral support from former co-workers. And Schmidt knows all this.No sooner has he eased into retirement, fate drops a bombshell in his lap. His wife Helen suddenly drops dead of a blood clot to her brain. Schmidt's daughter Jeannie (Hope Davis) comes for the funeral (but not his retirement party), and she's brought along her fiancée Randall Hertzell (Dermot Mulroney).Randall's a simpering loser of a water-bed salesman, and Schmidt doesn't approve of they're impending nuptials at all. Jeannie doesn't care. She advises Schmidt to only show up on the wedding day and do nothing else for this wedding (except write out a few cheques from time to time).Left all alone, Schmidt begins to reexamine his life. He senses his life has meant nothing to no-one, and the film itself is a confirmation of that belief. He freely admits Helen grew to annoy him constantly. They're marriage was over long before she died. His daughter has almost completely cut him out of her life. And his replacement at work doesn't need his advice. Schmidt's life is meaningless.He shares these confidences with Ndugu, a Tanzanian pen-pal he chooses to sponsor. Ndugu is only a six year old boy. It seems highly unlikely he could understand Schmidt's problems. But he needs to unburden his distress to someone. Anyone willing to listen.Schmidt decides to take the Winnebago (bought at Helen's request) and embark on a trip across country to look up his long forgotten past.I really loved About Schmidt. Alexander Payne's direction is right on the money. The film is filled with cute little throwaway gags. Little details packed away into every frame. And something new is revealed to you with each and every viewing.It's not only a gently amusing story. Its quite moving too. I'm not sure if Schmidt is on a journey of self-discovery. He already senses the truth about himself. I think he goes off on this little adventure to confirm what he fears about himself. But its all brought beautifully into perspective by Jack Nicholson.Nicholson has never been better. He adopts a minimalist mode of acting, which I think is about right for the character of Schmidt. Its all the more remarkable considering what an annoyingly over the top actor Nicholson can be at times. He downplays his emotions, and hides years of pain and frustration behind Schmidt's expressively wrinkled face. He never steps out of character.Each of Schmidt's stops on the road are like landmarks of his own personal road map. The home he lived in as a kid is gone. He meets a couple who represent the happiness he never had with Helen. And these vignettes are all wonderful. They highlight everything wrong with Schmidt's life, brilliantly underplayed by Nicholson.Then comes the inspired second half. Jeannie's wedding. Its at this point we meet Randall's mother Roberta, brought fantastically to life by Kathy Bates.She's the extrovert to Schmidt's introvert. She takes an instant liking to him, even though he fears Jeannie's life will sadly come to mirror his own if the marriage comes to pass. Roberta either doesn't see this, or doesn't want to. Its a beautifully modulated performance on Bates' part. Charming and ingratiating with Schmidt. Harsh and uncompromising with her family. And her scenes with Schmidt are eerily similar to her most famous character Annie Wilkes from Misery. The hot tub scene will stay with you long after the film is over.There's no ending of false optimism. Schmidt doesn't prevent the wedding. He knows Jeannie has made a serious mistake with Randall, but its too late to do anything about it now. There's too much bad blood between Schmidt and his daughter. He'll have to learn to accept that.The final scene is my favourite because its a moment of wonderful ambiguity. A letter from Ndugu reveals he can't read or write. A nun on his behalf sent a reply and a drawing he did. Schmidt bursts into tears. It's a beautiful scene because it opens itself up to a number of interpretations. Is he touched, distraught he poured out his heart for nothing, or overcome finally by his personal failures?About Schmidt leaves it to you to decide.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Just About</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When you look back at your life and you try to decide whether you accomplished anything after half a century of working, hopefully your answer will be both truthful and positive. Jack Nicholson's Warren R. Schmidt gives a decidedly untruthful and negative answer (to himself) in About Schmidt, an excruciatingly sad and painfully unfunny comedy.And yet, if you're willing to expend two-plus hours of time and patience, you're going to experience a wickedly dark satire about Schmidt, the middle-class company man whose retirement is as empty as his past.What makes AS worth the effort is the layering on of believable or forgivably satirical characters and events that lead Schmidt to an end-of-the-movie realization that he has, in fact, done something good and decent, that his life hasn't been a failure on every level except financial.My wife gave up on the story because she saw Schmidt as being so craven and bleak as to be declared beyond salvage. She's usually the one who has faith in humanity (I have embraced my inner misanthrope), but Nicholson's quintessentially anal and unforgiving Schmidt was a total loss to her. I agreed, but then there are the final moments of the movie, when Schmidt is confronted with a positive effect from a guilt-ridden cause he takes up early in the film.It's a sweet bit of forgiveness that will hopefully allow him to embrace his daughter's new family, an eclectic pack of working-class mutts and, in his words, nincompoops.Speaking of the nincompoopers, I have one complaint about Kathy Bates (and it isn't the cheap shot about how she drops--and droops--trou). Her badgering of ex-husband Howard Hesseman is so out of character, so nasty and Schmidt-like that it almost ruins an appreciation of her embodiment (and yikes, what a body; there, I got in my cheap shot) of a non-judgmental, hippie-dippy, sensual-being who welcomes Nicholson into her family with unconditional love.So, should you expend your time on About Schmidt? I'd say yes, but understand that, much like Nicholson's other retirement story, The Pledge, there isn't a clear ending, the payoff isn't in crisp, new bills. If anything, you'll be paid small, like in pennies.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>1970-2002</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1970, Jack Nicholson solidly established himself as an anti-heroic star in "Five Easy Pieces", about a man in search of himself. In 2002 - when I was as old as my parents were in 1970 - he came full circle in "About Schmidt", as another character in search of himself. And in a way, this movie seems to take it a step further: whereas in the first one, he was a young man (that is, the younger generation disillusioned by society's mores) who had deliberately wasted his life, in this one, he's an elderly man (who seemingly has long since stopped caring about whether or not he goes along with society's mores) who suddenly realizes that he has not lived as he would have liked.Anyway, the movie casts him as Omaha resident Warren Schmidt, on the way to Denver to attend his daughter's (Hope Davis) wedding. His drive to Denver looks to be the defining moment of his whole life, especially with a certain scene involving Kathy Bates (which was apparently the only scene that a lot of people remember).I try to imagine seeing "Five Easy Pieces" when it first came out, and then seeing "About Schmidt" 32 years later. One would realize that even as a member of the rebellious new generation (rejecting the old generation's wasteful lifestyle), things would eventually come back around. I get the feeling that maybe in a number of years, my generation will look like the people who wasted their entire lives. If so, then I can only hope to have fun while possible.All in all, a really good movie.</fullReview>

		<username>David Ziegler</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The danger of conformity</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>About Schmidt was a darkly comic cautionary tale about conforming. All of the details and characters in the movie are so typical of the everyday life around us (and a part of us?), so stripped of glamor, that it was at times almost painful to watch. At other times, that gritty realism, that lightly cynical coating over everything, was funny.One such moment was when Warren Schmidt is talking to the funeral director to make arrangements for his deceased wife, is told the price of a hearse, and asks, "What if I drive her myself?" Another has to do with Warren's hatred of his wife's collection of Hummel figurines, and his changing perspective on them after she dies. But this scene cannot be adequately related with words.The nice thing is that the movie is extremely accurate in its portrayal of everyday, bad-taste, unremarkable life. Usually movies like this end up laughing at such people from without--creating a caricature and ridiculing. This movie doesn't exaggerate, doesn't create cardboard clichés, and doesn't shield us from empathizing with the unlovely. There are even some tiny, brief but bright moments of touching sincerity that help buoy the film up. While the director clearly sees Schmidt as a tragedy, we also understand that he likes him. And we like him.It's worth the rental price to see Jack Nicholson so transformed. A good deal can be accomplished with makeup, but in many scenes he holds his mouth so wide that he looks like a bug. The opening scene, with Warren sitting in his office chair waiting for the minute hand on the clock to hit 5:00 so he can retire, is one such moment. Warren Schmidt is so opposite from the over-the-top Jack we know and love that it is a subtle but masterful testimony to Nicholson's talent.I haven't figured out if the movie will help me avoid falling into ruts, or whether it makes me feel smothered with the whispered thought that I'm no different. Immediately afterward, I think it had got under my skin. But with a little distance now, my appreciation of the movie seems to be rising steadily. As powerfully as Adaptation--but by showing us its exact opposite--the movie celebrates originality. It celebrates the road less traveled.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Better than "Sideways"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alexander Payne first hit the radar screen with "Election" a few years back, an arch, smart little comedy that I respected more than I liked. When "About Schmidt" came out, I was interested but somehow managed to miss it. I finally got around to viewing it on a cable channel last evening.In a word, "About Schmidt" is spectacular. Each scene is a finely-crafted, understated gem. Part of what I loved about the film is that Payne obviously decided to play it deadpan. It would have been remarkably easy to make Nicholson's character of Warren Schmidt an object of ridicule, and while that might have made a funnier movie, it wouldn't have had near the resonance they wound up with here.Nicholson truly has the performance of his career here. His Warren Payne is utterly ordinary, even banal, but this only makes him that much easier with which to identify. He plays Warren without a hint of irony.I marvel at storytellers who can tell big, important stories by relating a series of small, ordinary events, especially when they can do so without pulling those events out of shape to heighten the drama. Much of this film looks like a documentary. Warren's speech at his daughter's wedding is given no false weight, and yet it winds up forming an utterly natural climax for this story.I liked "Sideways", Payne's subsequent film, but like "Election" it fell just a bit short of the mark for me. This is maybe another example of Hollywood rewarding an artist for a previous work that fell through the cracks. I would honestly call "About Schmidt" a great American movie.</fullReview>

		<username>mdm-11</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Old man in search of life's purpose</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson is brilliant as ever as an "average guy" who suddenly faces multiple major crises: After some 40 years of service to an insurance giant, his final day at work is ticking away (literally). Now retired, a life of leasure and fun should begin. WRONG! Within weeks his wife of 42 years dies suddenly, leaving him to "look forward" to their only daughter's wedding to a total loser. "Schmidt" manages to face everything with dignity and without causing any trouble. Still, the man who tried to do everything in his life right, by the book, and with all consideration for everyone else, feels unwanted, unloved, as though his life didn't matter at all, he had lived for absolutely no purpose.If this had been "it", the story would be quite depressing. Fortunately there is a bittersweet "point" to it all. As Schmidt embarks on "married life after retirement", a TV ad asking for people to "sponsor a needy child" in a developing country, catches his attention. Schmidt responds to the call for help, and begins a "diary-like" correspondence with his foster son, a 6 year old orphan in Africa, whose photo he received from the relief organization. As though writing regular entries into a journal, Schmidt tells this boy all of his troubles, along with sharing fond memories and things happening at the time. The therapeutic value of this letter writing becomes evident in the final scene, which is heartbreaking."About Schmidt" is not fast-paced, but the DVD version explains that many scenes were shortened or cut entirely for time. All of the remaining scenes are somehow relevant to the story. The final "message" is worth following the at times dragging storyline. Nicholson (of course) and Kathy Bates in the small role as the "mother-in-law" are wonderful. Their teaming alone is worth watching this film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Misery Needs Company</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alexander Payne's 2002 film, About Schmidt, is just as much the transformation of it's star Jack Nicholson as it is the transformation of the character Jack plays "Warren Schmidt." Here Nicholson is no longer the swaggering, smart alecked, tough guy we have seen throughout his long, illustrious career but a quiet, shell of a nice guy trying to understand his life after retirement. Nicholson does such an amazing job portraying this loser of a character one wonders why he hasn't been playing this type all along.The movie begins with the retirement of Warren Schmidt. He is a typical Midwestern "good guy" who is retiring as an executive from an insurance company. Schmidt is an everyman schlub. He has worked hard to have a "normal" life. He has a good job, a long standing wife, and a nice daughter. Yet upon retiring, the death of a loved one, and his daughters imminent marriage Schmidt must take a harder look at his life. In doing so he comes to realize there isn't much to it, really. The bulk of the movie centers on Schmidt traveling to Colorado to try to stop his daughter from marrying a redneck boob.Theerer are numerous perfect spoofs of Midwestern living. From Schmidt's life, to his retirement plans of living in a trailer the details of a typical Midwestern life are just about perfect. While on the road, Schmidt stops at numerous Interstate museums that are so banal it is hilarious. Once he arrives in Colorado the characterizations of the fiancée and his family are both hilarious and frighteningly real. Dermot Mulroney plays the mullett wearing, salesman fiancée and Kathy Bathes plays the still living in the 60's time of free love soon to be mother-in-law. The family dinner before the wedding is reminiscent of real life, mixing hilarity and sadness with the eye of an artist. The actual wedding is so dead on perfect that I believe I have actually attended that very ceremony. From the off key singing of the schmaltzy "Longer" to the self written vows (I will love you every day, and when I say day I mean all 24 hours, and when I say hours I mean...) the ceremony is hilarious in its real life cheezeball hokeyness and yet manages to remain as sweet.This is what makes the film so memorable. While it pokes fun and satirizes everyday Midwestern life it is full of rich glowing love for that very life. Schmidt is an normal schmuck who has lived his life by the rules. While at the end of his life he begins to regret that simple life, I don't believe the film is suggesting that this type of life is meaningless. Just the opposite in fact, I believe it is showing all of us everyday schmucks that living a normal life can be glorious in its own way when we help those around us.</fullReview>
	</review>

Schmidt, a recently retired widower experiencing a late-life identity crisis. Writer/director Alexander Payne has a gift for wringing great lines and big laughs out of sad situations. These aren't the high energy, more obvious gags of, say, the Farrelly brothers- but make no mistake, ABOUT SCHMIDT is a comedy, and a magnificent one.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Fine piece of cinema, well made</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson is Warren Schmidt in this bitter-sweet 'comedy' about coping in late middle-age and being plagued by your demons. After retiring, then losing his wife he embarks on a road trip towards his daughter's home in Denver where she is about to get married to a man Schmidt holds in a great deal of contempt. Ultimately the movie's story isn't actually that coherent, but it's supposed to be that way. It's not really so much about the events themselves as the way Schmidt's life is affected by them, and Jack conveys Warren's emotions supremely well. He's immeasurably believable.But Nicholson's acting isn't the only commendable performance; all actors in this are faultless. The script calls for a quirky edge without being ridiculous, and we get that extremely effectively from them all.We get inside Warren's head via his reading out, mentally, of letters he writes to a poor child in the 3rd world he's sponsoring, and tells the child the bad aspects of his life as well as the good.It's this attention to detail, as well as Nicholson's outstanding portrayal, which means we really get inside Warren's soul. His entire being is on display here, which makes this a quite personal film.Many reviews, such as the blurb on the box, wax lyrical about the comedy aspect, but really I'd suggest it's a light-hearted drama with deeper overtones. There are no belly laughs, that's for sure. While there are amusing moments, it's never really a *funny* film.Yes, what carries it are the performances, particularly Nicholson's. It's a finely made movie, and worthy of your time.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-01-05</date>
		<username>FilmOtaku (ssampon@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Nicholson shines in this multi-layered film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm not entirely sure exactly what was keeping me from seeing `About Schmidt' for so long. Misunderstanding of the premise? Lack of education about the film? I was under the impression that this film, directed by Alexander Payne (who also brought us `Election - a fantastic film) was another sweet comedy abut the life of Middle Aged America.**Spoilers Within**Well, obviously I was wrong on several levels. About Schmidt is probably one of the saddest films I have ever seen that wasn't in the standard `tear jerker' category. The film tells the tale of Warren Schmidt, a man in his 60's who is forced to retire from his job, realizes that he has `grown tired' of his wife and has to contend with the fact that his beloved only daughter is about to marry a complete moron. When his wife unexpectedly dies, he decides to take the obligatory deluxe motor home he and his wife had purchased to enjoy their `golden years' and takes off across the country to try to stop his daughter's wedding.The story sounds simple enough when you outline it on paper like I just did, which is probably one of the reasons I avoided it for so long. What it is however is an incredibly multi-layered story that shows the `other', perhaps more real side of retirement. We don't see Schmidt elated that he is now retired and has free time; we seem him going back to the company mere days after his last day, hoping to get a bite of any kind of project from his much younger successor. We don't see Schmidt and his wife frolicking on the beach like the retirees we see on television. We see their bored, habitual relationship that has an enormous amount of strain because they have surpassed the `passion' level and long ago entered `routine tinged with irritation'. Even something as cliché as trekking across the country in a motor home is made different by the sights visited and the experiences had. Additionally, Schmidt is such a seemingly emotionally closed-off man that sometimes the only way we as the audience definitely know what is going on in his head or what his true feelings are is through the letters he sends to his `adopted' sponsor child, Ntugu. Since Schmidt sees things mostly as black or white, and took the `suggestion' to write to your child seriously and possibly because he needs an outlet to discuss his feelings, we hear his innermost thoughts about everything in his life. It isn't until he indirectly hears back from Ntugu at the end that we finally see him really break down and allow himself to cry.All of these elements make for a fantastic and intriguing story, but it is Nicholson that gives Schmidt life and heart. When I watched the film a month ago, I proclaimed him to be one of the saddest characters I have ever seen, and upon reflection time has not swayed that opinion. His appearance, his quiet demeanor and even his shuffling walk all make Schmidt a sad man, and Nicholson played the part absolutely brilliantly. It is a given that Nicholson is going to be good in anything that he does - he is in the same league as Hackman, where they can pretty much utter a line and people are going to line up to give him a nomination of some sort. But Nicholson, at least in his last dozen films or so has relied on playing the role of the scoundrel or the playboy - pretty much what he projects as his public persona as well. Schmidt is an entirely different role for him, and he was astonishingly good, and unbelievably convincing. Actresses are sometimes lauded for `not being afraid to play their age' - which, in these image conscious times is a valid compliment. Nicholson should be praised in the same light for his role in this film.About Schmidt should be a must-see film and will probably garner many different reactions from many different people. The few people with whom I have discussed the film that have actually seen it did not feel the profound sadness I felt. Of course, there were some comedic moments, but the film is so touching and so thought-provoking that it is impossible to categorize it in a general sense, but I would give it one label - Excellent.--Shelly</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Some of my friends were very disappointed with About Schmidt. It's a downer. Jack looks like the same old Jack, but Warren Schmidt is not a funny man, he's a shell of a human. A recently retired life insurance underwriter in Omaha, his wife abruptly dies leaving him alone and with nothing do to. His wife and his job were his whole universe, and now they're both gone. To make matters worse, while going through his wife's affects, he finds love letters from a long ago adulterous affair with their best friend. In late middle age, what little life Warren has remaining comes crashing down around him.His grown daughter is getting married in Colorado (to a man Warren believes beneath her), so Warren embarks on a road trip to see her and meet her family. A family headed by Roberta, played by Kathy Bates, who embraces life. Will she be Warren's salvation?Warren Schmidt is a very difficult character, but Jack Nicholson nails it with a very understated and gutsy performance. He is supported by a great and believable supporting cast, and the on-site Midwestern winter locales add truthfulness to the tale. And then there's Jack in one of his best roles. About Schmidt is highly recommended.</fullReview>
