		<username>JasonJayDelmonico</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Jacko's still got it!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Final Score: 6.0 (out of 10)***Possible Spoilers***With his last movie, the brilliant, subtle, acerbic "Election", Alexander Payne made a name for himself with me by using a style that was sarcastic, smug, but generally objective about it's lead characters without any clear heroes or villains. The movie seemed to be told in 3rd person from many points of view and, while it threw the L-word around a lot, there (intentionally so) didn't seem to be a genuine moment in the movie. Payne stood at a distance, satirizing everything. Here we have "About Schmidt", Payne's follow-up, where he appears to be trying to prove that he has a heart. He dives right into this story and wallows around in Schmidt's feelings of loneliness, isolation and shallow induced sadness. The story is fairly basic, but this is an actor's movie and it's most effective moments are the one's where we just follow Jack Nicholson around as he revisits his past and tours roadside historical landmarks. It also feature stellar turns from Hope Davis (who I love), Kathy Bates and a hilarious Dermot Mulroney as the quintessential hillbilly- complete with balding mullet, goatee and get rich quick pyramid scheme plans. The movie is also at it's high when Schmidt writes to his 6 year old adopted foster child from a 3rd world country, Ndugu. The letters are funny in the sense that Schmidt speaks well over Ndugu's head (swearing and talking about insurance and pledging fraternities), but there is a real sweetness to it: Schmidt is giving this unloved child fatherly advice. It's that cuteness and sense of realism and honesty that make "About Schmidt" watchable.However, as I mentioned, the performances clank around in a generally unimaginative and predictable film. It's tone swings wildly from syrupy sweet to sarcastic or oddball. It is a sad portrait of a sad man in a generally sad movie- the kind of portrait we have seen in abundance recently from "American Beauty" to "Fight Club". The defining moment in the film is the payoff in the final scene which will either win you over or turn you off. To me it was completely predictable and something, had Schmidt not been so shallow, he should have gotten much earlier. It makes the whole film seem like a commercial for adopting foster children, but Nicholson still pulls it off (which is why I admire him so much in this film- it's easy to be good in a really good movie) and sweeps you up with the expressions on his face and the look in his eyes. Still, staring at that picture and watching Schmidt's face and reviewing the events that just occured, I couldn't help but feel manipulated. Doesn't matter anyway, when "About Schmidt" is over all anyone is going to be talking about is Kathy Bate's gutsy nude scene.</fullReview>

		<username>lmitrani</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Nicholson Portrays Bleak, Heartfelt Retiree In Sturdy Performance</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>About Schmidt is an extraordinary film, and we have Jack Nicholson to thank for it. We're grateful not just for his excellent performance, but for achieving what movie moguls consider unthinkable. Jack, now age 65, shows that a character who is a retiree, a senior citizen, can star in a marketable film. He reveals and deals with all aspects of aging: the extra weight, sagging skin, reduced libido, and diminished capacity to enjoy life. (Many of these attributes can be diminished, if not eliminated, if we try.)The circumstances of losing a spouse, struggling with independence after a long marriage, grown children wanting to separate from parents, and disillusionment from retiring are painfully believable. Schmidt's thoughts are very clear since he narrates the film, including his sponsorship of an African boy and his refreshing cross-country trip across Kansas &amp; Nebraska. Nicholson fans will be satisfied to see that his well-known cynicism and irony reveal themselves in his narration. His characterization of Schmidt ? a retired insurance executive who appears in every scene and is almost devoid of emotion -- is a complete departure from the volatile, explosive personality he usually portrays.About Schmidt challenges us to explore whether a corporate management job is meaningful, what makes life worthwhile, and whether parents' intervening in the lives of grown children is meddling or helpful advice. Despite the movie's capacity to wear us down with weighty issues, it entertains us by introducing fascinating characters and unpredictable situations. It ends on an appropriately poignant note, with scattered audience applause. We see that the formula found in most box-office mega hits -- violence, special effects, young photogenic stars, characters easily identifiable as good or bad, and a happy ending with lovers uniting or villains vanquished ? can be turned aside for more nourishing entertainment. A good story and candid dialogue still matter, and Schmidt delivers on both counts. As someone who grew up admiring Jack Nicholson as a counter-culture figure in Easy Rider, I find his ability to still command center stage inspirational. At least in this film, the coveted 25-49 media demographic plays a secondary role to Jack and his near contemporary, Kathy Bates. It's hard to imagine a lesser actor or personality pulling this off.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Alexander Payne allows Jack to be a dull boy.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Back in his familiar Midwest, after tackling such life-and-death issues as abortion in Citizen Ruth and high school politics in Election, Alexander Payne wanders through the flat landscapes as he follows an ordinary man's journey of self discovery. The director's latest, About Schmidt takes its time to develop as it introduces us to Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson), who at 66 is retiring from a lifetime of service as an actuary, is coping with his wife's sudden death and has to stomach his only daughter's marriage to a halfwit. Schmidt's excellent adventure aboard his 35-foot motor home climaxes in an impassioned attempt to stop the wedding after meeting the groom's boorish family. Kathy Bates is a hoot as the free-spirited mother-in-law, but this is all about Jack. His understated performance is heartbreaking as he embraces his physical maturity to solidify the character's distress. His crossroads happen to be Midwestern in kind, but Payne's sharp comedy reaches beyond the plains.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-01-08</date>
		<username>Andrew Howard (lhseaglerunner@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>"About" a non-surprising surprise</shortDescription>
		<fullReview> In the course of critiquing films, one is bound to stumble across one so shocking and so unexpected that it leaves the reviewer gasping for words. This was something of the case after I saw `About Schmidt,' but in other ways this description is the farthest thing from the truth. The end of the film is a mundane and non-surprising, but the element it gives is nothing but a surprise. Maybe I'm babbling here, but follow along and maybe you will see my point. Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson) is bored. Having recently retired as an actuary at a major Omaha insurance company, only to be replaced by a young nitwit, Schmidt is questioning his entire life, including Helen (June Squibb), his wife of forty-two years who he professes to have woken up and wondered `Who is this woman sleeping next to me?' Schmidt's only consolation and relief to this new found life of retired `ease' is Ndugu, a Tanzanian orphan that he has `adopted' for $22 a month and has taken to writing long epistles detailing his fears. But Schmidt's orderly life is turned upside down when his wife suddenly dies. This tragedy causes him to set off on a roadtrip through the Great Plains and his soul to Denver where his daughter, Jeannie (Hope Davies) is preparing to get married to Randall Herzel (Dermot Mulroney), a waterbed salesman with a balding mullet (you'll see). In Denver, he will have a, uh, personal encounter with his future son-in-law's nutty mother (Kathy Bates), which adds to the impact on a life of order. What amazed me most about `About Schmidt,' is director Alexander Payne's use of silence. It sounds crazy, but in this world of sound, the occasional meditative aura of silence improves this film by leaps and bounds because it is a technical trick seen in a very few other movies of the modern era. But the silence would not be nearly as effective without Jack Nicholson as the star. To just go into a cinema to watch Nicholson's facial expressions is an experience that ANY theater buff or prospective actor should do. Nicholson, without a doubt, carries the film like few other actors of his, or any other generation could aptly do. Along with Jack, actress Kathy Bates gives a standout performance. She is able to juxtapose herself with the taciturn Nicholson that runs the gamut from the extroverted Roberta (her character) to the introvert Schmidt. I also grudgingly have to give props to Bates for her courage. Why, you ask? Well, you need to see it to believe it. There is one other thing of note that I mustn't fail to mention-the unique plot device of the voiceover letter. Clearly the highlight of the film, the several letters that Schmidt writes to his African sponsored child are some of the funniest works of writing that I have ever seen. Truly, the entire audience cracks up to hear the familiar beginning to words of wisdom, `Dear Ndugu?' `About Schmidt' is all about modern philosophy and satire. This is a movie about ordinary people doing fairly ordinary things and living fairly ordinary lives as they try to impact the lives of others. Philosophy speaks through the film that the need to impact other's lives is a needed part of the soul, which provides food for thought from an arena better known for food from the popper. I liked this film a lot, but it unsettled me at times, but not enough to prevent my bestowing a 9.5 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Like many people, I expected that "About Schmidt" would be a great film, out-doing the greatness of "Gangs of New York" and "Lord of the Rings".I was sadly mistaken. Not even the exceptional talents of Jack Nicholson could have improved the meandering script, the uninteresting relationships, and the overdone plot.I have seen and read about enough old men, who having lived their lives without any thought or merit, try to make up for it all-and this film makes a little African boy's picture enough to reconcile this problem for Schmidt. Ultimately, this film adds nothing new to this exploration of the human condition, it lies about life and tries to make the audience accept it.In an attempt to give Warren Schmidt some meaning and direction for his life, the script has him run into all sorts of the things, like a commercial asking for donations to help feed Poor and Starving African children, or a box of letters proving that his recently-deceased wife was having an affair with his best friends. First of all-who cares? Second of all, I feel cheated that the writer would create a story out of such arbitrary occurrences, rather than penetrating the faults and deep emotions of the main character. This film was script driven, not plot or character driven-but it kept trying to pretend that this was life happening.As for Jack, I did say that he has exceptional talent as an actor. Yes, he can convey a wide array of emotions with a twitch of his brow and a blink of his eye-but if there is no story giving any of this relevance, what value does it have? And for the record-what was the story? Schmidt finding the meaning of his life in helping an African child? Trying to make amends to his estranged daughter? Learning what a selfish fool he has been and doing something about it? The script refuses to focus on anything, and all these elements suffer.I walked away feeling that Schmidt's life was even more pointless at the end of the film than at the beginning, but the film wanted us to think that all of his troubles somehow amounted to something, especially with that run-of-the-mill drawing by Ndugu. Bathetic to the lowest degree! Sorry, folks. That won't work for me. You cannot make me believe the exact opposite of what the screen is showing. And Jack's tearful ending makes no difference.Last of all, seeing Kathy Bates "au naturel" was unecessary, and the merit or lack thereof of the fiancé was so muddled, I just gave up trying to understand.American cinema has got to do better than pretend to be artistic.Jack, find better work, or quit while you're ahead.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I have seen many 'User Comments' whine and cry about many different movies and how they have propaganda written all over them. 99% of them have been far from the truth, yet ABOUT SCHMIDT would be a reasonable complaint that propaganda could be started from this movie. I remember in RKO 281, Orson Welles said that "people watch movies to watch themselves," or something along those lines. ABOUT SCHMIDT has that effect, though many people when they pursue a normal life and then hit retirement may look back at themselves like this. Jack Nicholson portrays a brilliant character study in a movie that will doubtfully make big numbers at the box office due to its views.After 42 years of work, Warren Schmidt has retired. What do you do?There are a few problems with this movie. First off, could you call it a movie? ABOUT SCHMIDT takes so many breaks to study Warren that it can become draining. The supporting cast saves us from joining Warren in his desperate search for himself. Secondly, the idea of a character study does not appeal to everyone, and despite Nicholson's positively brilliant performance (his monologue during the wedding was excellent, as was soul searching in the stars), ABOUT SCHMIDT feels long. There is no bad acting in this movie, but I can already feel dissenting voices against the critics who love Schmidt. It is hardly an uplifting experience, but ABOUT SCHMIDT will not betray anyone who loves Jack's study on human behavior. It has a nice ending.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>About JACK!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is one of the best of the year. It is directed with a quiet ambiance and stillness by Alexander Payne that reflects the situation affecting its main character. Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson) has just retired from the insurance business and his wife suddenly passes away. His daughter is about to marry a man he doesn't approve of and throughout all of this, he looks back on his life and feels like he's accomplished very little or nothing and that his contribution to the world will be insignificant. Based on the novel by Louis Begley, Alexander Payne and Jim Taylor have crafted an adapted screenplay full of ordinary and yet powerful observations on life. This is a little like an 'American Beauty' on geriatrics only with a more sympathetic feeling. Very little music score is used and the contributions of the supporting role including Kathy Bates, Hope Davis and Dermot Mulroney are terrific as well. This is a picture for those who REALLY appreciate the art of film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A modern-day Everyman</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"About Schmidt" is cinema at its best, what moviemaking should be all about. Jack Nicholson (as Warren Schmidt) is a modern-day Everyman. Like all of us, he spends a lifetime pursuing a career and fearing he has nothing to show for it, caring for a wife and family and still doubting that he has made a lasting impact. He sees all of the people and things he valued slip from his grasp, and we share his loss, bewilderment, grief and pain. Kathy Bates serves as a perfect foil for Nicholson, with her portrayal of a woman with a healthy appetite for life and a no-nonsense attitude that is diametrically opposed to the quiet rage and flat emotionless response of Nicholson's character. In the end, Schmidt finds new meaning in life and a new way to contribute to the world. His triumph, as much as his tragedy, is ours too. When the lights go up on "About Schmidt", we are all uplifted and redeemed.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Redemption comes in teaspoons not cups</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There were so many truths in this movie that paralleled my own recent family experiences. Warren Schmidt's life was his work; his wife an aging stranger who occupies his home and whose snoring disturbs his quiet. He is completely unable to take care of himself or his home when he retires and his wife dies. There's a whole world out there which he doesn't understand because his life was his work and his wife managed everything else for him. He finds himself lonesome for someone whom he probably never really knew.Here's where the subtle optimism of this movie comes into play. When Schmidt can no longer bear the squalor of his widowed life, he goes on a road trip in his Winnebago to visit his daughter, hoping that she'll take care of him as her late mother did. He futilely attempts to find some meaning or purpose for his life. He attempts to retrace his childhood. He misinterprets the sympathy of a stranger for sexual advances. He journals his feelings in letters he writes to a 6-year old African child whom he sponsors. He ultimately acquires a tiny bit of humanity, grace and perhaps redemption.Leaving the movie I heard a woman remark that she didn't like the ending of the movie. Although I didn't ask her what she meant, my guess is that she didn't find it upbeat or optimistic enough. I would have told her this: most of us leave the earth with little insight into our lives and ourselves. We all-too-rarely make amends, tell others how we feel about them, or take stock of our lives. Perhaps redemption only comes in teaspoon-size, about the size of Schmidt's tears.</fullReview>

		<username>giancarlorocks</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A Coming of Age film, without the 'Coming of Age'...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview> Director Alexander Payne first caught my eye with his 1999 eye opener?Election'. Matthew Broderick and an undiscovered Reese Witherspoon starredin that fine film dealing with conflict and pride. In his newest outing, Payne directs the sublime Jack Nicholson in one of his richest roles and conflict and pride are also brought to the forefront in a delightful little film entitled ?About Schmidt'. Payne engineers a rich screenplay which stems from a novel by LouisBegley. Payne's screenplay allows Nicholson to show a wide array of rangeand depth as his Warren Schmidt is a character befuddled by inner angst and a lack of true satisfaction with life. Payne sets the story in his hometown ofOmaha,Nebraska ( One of the Theatre Marquis in Omaha showed the title for?Sideways', Payne's yet to be released Project). and uses the simplistic setting to retell the complicated story of a retired Assistant Vice President of anInsurance carrier who must deal with his abrupt end to normality.  Or so one thinks. As the film unravels, we are introduced to a myriad of characters and dilemmas as Warren Schmidt makes us understand themonotony and tediousness of his life. Payne opens the film with a smart little series of shots displaying the building where Schmidt works. After a quirky few shots presenting the high rise building as the centerpiece in the middle of a small town, Payne thrusts the viewer right into the empty office of a lonesome Schmidt counting down the seconds until his retirement. Without saying a word for the first three minutes of the film, we can already feel the loneliness and discontent of this man. Payne unravels his characters at a ?snails pace' intentionally so the audience can appreciate and understand the complexities of these well writtencharacters. Schmidt's biggest complexity is his unhappiness. The characterdeliciously incarnated by Jack Nicholson is a treat to watch as he wrestles with his inner angst. Payne's screenplay tackles issues many grapple with on aregular basis: Have I made a difference in the world? Was my life worthanything? Did I lead a good life? All these questions arise when Nicholson retires and is forced to travel the US to celebrate his daughter's wedding. Without giving too much away, thescript is just superb. The character of Warren Schmidt is a sad and insecureindividual who for all of his life despite his wife, or his child, has never really spoken with anyone. Payne uses an unconventional outlet for Schmidt to vent,for not even his adulterous best friend can be turned to anymore. Schmidt'scharacter seemed cold and frail, but he warmed up to an overseas child foster care program. For twenty two dollars a month, Schmidt was not only able to help a child in Tanzania, but he was able to help himself as those letters sent to his foster child were testaments of his repressed good nature and dire loneliness, Some hurdles discovered along the way are close deaths within the family, an idiotic future son in law and a daughter he would do anything in the world for. Payne paints a miraculous portrait of appreciation and zest for life and does so in at times a quite comedic manner without being overbearingly funny. Payne directs the film with a touch of class as he presents us with a fresh and awe inspring tale of insipdness and hostility for one's life. It is a coming of age film without the coming of age. It simply tells the story of a man searching for inner peace. It shows us how easy it is to get caught up in our careers, in our successes, that many tend to forget the riches far past those of monetary value. The film is a remarkable journey into a thorough soul searching session and additional marvelous performances add to the anti-generic film that it is. Kathy Bates, Howard Hesseman and Dermot Mulroney add to the brilliance of this film. A categorically great film for anyone who has dealt with crises of their own and wants to see how other would deal with their troubles.Giancarlo's Rating: ***</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>"About Schmidt" presents a squirmingly realistic portrait of an aging man whose connection to the human race is nonchalant at best, who learns through the course of the movie that one's connection to humanity is truly the only thing that actually matters in this world. Nicholson portrays Warren Schmidt with remarkable and uncanny restraint, knowing exactly how much emotion to invest into each response and reaction; it is a performance he's not given before (I generally think he plays himself in most of his movies and it's about time he's shown us something deeper, more profound.) This performance will most definitely earn him an Oscar nomination if not an actual Oscar, and the entire cast is brilliant as well, particularly Kathy Bates who will most likely get a Best Supporting Actress nomination. "About Schmidt" is not for everyone; a lot of people (particularly YOUNGER people) may find it torturous to watch, for all the wrong reasons - it IS torturous to watch but there's a magnificent pay-off, one of the best in any movie I've ever seen. So hang in and endure. You can do it.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>For years, Jack Nicholson has been one of my favorite actors. From One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest to Heartburn to As Good As It Gets, he has never ceased to amaze me, and he has never disappointed me. Now, as he has "slowed down", along comes About Schmidt; a film where Nicholson takes a low-key role and plays a man who is trying to fix the life he feels he has wasted, period. No strings attached. Nicholson is playing a relatively normal guy, without having to be a psychotic or a womanizing older man. And he does it beautifully. I would have to say that About Schmidt is a one-man show, containing great backup characters for Jack. I don't have much more to say about this movie, although I was very impressed. I guess you could compare Schmidt to some aspects of the quirkier As Good As It Gets, but I'd have to say that this is a kinder, gentler Nicholson. 		<username>Lois-R</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A movie for grown-ups that packs an emotional wallop</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson shines in his sensitive, tour de force performance as Warren Schmidt, the vice president of an insurance company who finds retirement anything but fulfilling. In fact, his world starts to crumble in short order, along with his relationships, his priorities and his sanity. A superficial reading would pigeonhole Schmidt as a Willy Loman retread, minus the heart condition, but Alexander Payne plumbs deeper emotional currents with this wonderful film - the sort of film that reminded me why I go to movies in the first place.To the director's credit, the film never quite crosses the line (so common in Hollywood these days) of ridiculing its characters and their sensibilities. Make no mistake: Midwestern middle-class values go under the magnifying glass, but just when the viewer starts to feel superior, zing! Payne pulls you back from the brink, and you find yourself caring deeply about Warren Schmidt and his universal predicament. As Warren drives his RV down the interstate, he seems as doomed as the cows his glimpses in the passing cattle trucks. Constantly thwarted in his attempts at communicating with his family and friends (by their indifference, by his unwillingness to hurt their feelings, or by the annoying demands of a voice-mail computer), he pours his heart out to someone he has never met. The editing, the supporting cast (especially Kathy Bates), and the cinematography are well-nigh perfect, and all this combined allows Nicholson to soar. The layers of his character, a man who sees the truth but dares not express it to those closest to him, erupt in a volcano of mixed emotions of anger, fear and despair by the film's last scenes and transcendent finale.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-12-26</date>
		<username>jdesando</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>No other film has better shown me how being prepared for retirement is a lifetime job.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>`About Schmidt," starring Jack Nicholson, is a credible reflection on middle class retirement and self-awareness. I am moved by the static images of this retired insurance man, staring off into the unknown, comprehending little of what life means and failing miserably to deal with the recent loss of his wife of 42 years. Don't look for Jack's patented wise-ass grin; his Warren Schmidt moves like a robot or a patient just off the surgery gurney--dazed and confused by life's indifference to him and his inability to make a difference. The core of "About Schmidt's" success is Nicholson's ability to wrench meaning out of the commonplace: a small wringing of hands, an awkward, limping shuffle. Only when he assumes command of his Winnebago does he seem at all relaxed. Yet he cannot escape the existential awareness journeys offer (Remember his journey in `Easy Rider'?), as when an RV camp lady says to him, "I see inside of you a sad man." Warren narrates through voice-over to his "adopted" son, a Tanzanian boy benefiting from Schmidt's $22 a month to a world charitable organization. Most of his letters to the child are inappropriately adult or insensitive to the child's impoverished, illiterate life. Yet the comments are a signal about how out of it Schmidt really is and how manipulative the child-care charities can be in getting a man's money. Schmidt's ineffectuality is best expressed in his misguided, recently acquired concern for his daughter and the dorky, pony-tailed salesman she is about to marry. Don't look for a Monsoon or Fat Greek wedding; this one (they play Dan Fogelberg's "Longer" and Paul Stookey's "Wedding Song') is as bland as Schmidt's phlegmatic retirement party. For both those events and his own personal life, Schmidt is an outrider. Director Alexander Payne, who caught the sad spirit of high school politics in "Election," triumphs here in directing a hero like us, lost in our thoughts, divorced from family and jobs, but good enough to carry on and maybe find some light while camping out on the top of the Winnebago.Nicholson's Schmidt is the most memorable and least glamorous protagonist this year in film. He should take home the Oscar honors with more awareness than poor Schmidt, who might not have seen a movie this year.No other film has better shown me how being prepared for retirement is a lifetime job. No other actor has played the man who wasn't prepared better than Jack (nor does Jack show signs of retirement himself). Thank goodness.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>In praise of civility</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One scene in "About Schmidt" epitomizes this wonderfully sly satire of Americana. Warren, the recent widower and frustrated soon to be father-of-the-bride, sits and observes the groom's family chomping at food as if they were grazing at the trough. His predicament is that his daughter (Hope Davis) is eating just like them. Jack Nicholson, as Warren, is in practically every scene and his dramatic and comedic talent have never been better showcased (the tearful widower in a wonderful monologue atop his travel van, the struggling water bed sleeper who soon shows the effects of too much Percodan, and the seemingly vindictive father who turns 180 degrees in his wedding speech) . He doesn't work with glamorous women like in "The Witches of Eastwick" and he has a future son-in-law (Dermot Mulroney) that he graciously calls a nincompoop. No, there is his very old looking wife of 42 years, Helen, who makes him use the toilet seat to urine and a domineering soon to be mother-in-law, Roberta (Kathy Bates), who doesn't mind talking about her breast feeding, her orgasms, nor does she mind indulging in a nice hot tub session with Warren. The film deliberately keeps the straight-laced, broad shouldered Warren as a one dimensional, penny-pincher (the pine box was too cheap for his wife but not the upgrade) who is unable to deal with his retirement, his estranged daughter's wedding, nor self-doubts about what he has really accomplished. Much like the Coen brothers' film, "Fargo", this film makes us look at ourselves from the microscopic detail of sagging skin to the macroscopic view of a man's small place in his universe. It goes further by showing the terrible irony of a lonely, frustrated man whose only joy in life is confiding in a six year old boy he has never seen, in a faraway country where for the cost of $22 a month, he can finally say he has redemption.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2002-12-22</date>
		<username>Chris Knipp</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Jack's back.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>`About Schmidt' reminds one why `American Beauty' seemed uplifting: it made us see an ordinary man's boring life as something he could toy with and perhaps escape from. Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicolson) makes feeble efforts to escape ? and once he sets sail in the Winnebago, he can always jump into it and drive off again. But he has no inner wildness to discover. Inside he has perhaps wellsprings of fear, sadness, and rage, but he's neither willing nor able to express them. To call Schmidt's fate in the movie an `existential crisis,' as some critics have done, is a bit of a stretch. `American Beauty' was Kevin Spacey's finest hour, if not his most challenging role. Opinions vary on Jack Nicolson's performance in `About Schmidt.' Is this the work of a great actor, or the wreck of one, or was he always a great obnoxious scene-stealing ham who's lucked into some terrific roles -- but here simply winks at the audience from behind the befuddled Babbitt he is playing? In `About Schmidt,' Nicolson surely has moments in which each of these facets appears: we see a great actor, and a burnt out one; a ham, and a celebrity hiding in a role that would be beneath him if it didn't rise to such sublimely nerve-wracking and embarrassing levels of tragicomedy. For the most part the hero of Alexander Payne's movie is simply a thinly disguised but magisterially reposeful Jack Nicolson who in scene after scene holds back or, as A. Lane memorably writes, `swinishly lolls.' And this is what the role calls for. One can debate the performance, but it's pretty clear that the cinematic Schmidt is largely a cipher. Inside every Schmidt, it appears, there is a Jack Nicolson violently gesturing to be let out -- but here exercising moments of cunning repression. For an actor as flamboyant as Nicolson to play with great restraint is in itself a form of flamboyance.Schmidt doesn't wish to face anything, and he doesn't. He tries to escape from almost every moment that calls for active presence. First it's by slipping off for a drink at a bar when his retirement party is going on. Then it's by jumping into the Winnebago when living alone gets rocky. He has shown himself pathetically unable to cope after his wife dies. He has turned against her and his best friend for a short flirtation between them long ago. And he has run away from it all. But in the end he only escapes into drugs (the Percodan Kathy Bates' character gives him for his twisted neck) and fantasies of a paternal relationship with a small African boy he buys foster care for. This is how he buys off his `existential crisis': he sends off monthly checks to Africa for $22. You could better call this not `existential crisis' but middle class white guilt. The voiceovers of Schmidt's letters to the boy are the only semblance of a sensibility that the movie offers, and its only resolution is validation of this boy's existence via a letter from a nun in the final scene. There is much sadness in this, but no enlightenment.`About Schmidt' indeed is a rather distressing (but nonetheless surefooted) example of what could be called Todd Solandz lite, which was found much more amusingly displayed in `Pumpkin' and `The Good Girl' earlier this year. This time the level of caricature is kept low enough and pervasive enough to be hardly ever funny. The movie offers a quarter of an hour of genuine amusement when Schmidt arrives at his daughter's future mother-in-law's house. Kathy Bates provides some relief from the emptiness of Schmidt's respectable, actuarial existence (he was in insurance in the director's own native Omaha). She swears; she yells at her ex. Her house has a dated hippy charm and the way her New Age sexuality threatens Schmidt is blissful because she also threatens for a moment to take over the screen with her warm presence. The bad taste of the décor is enormously welcome here after the Fifties colonial neutrality of Schmidt's residence; the dinner featuring the garrulous ex, rude Roberta, and airhead son Randall is a hoot. As Randall the handsome but ill fated Dermot Mulroney (he once seemed destined to become a matinee idol) comes supplied with a balding pate, a ridiculous set of whiskers, a long ponytail, and a job selling waterbeds ? a pretty heavy load for an actor who once squired Julia Roberts. `About Schmidt' quickly descends into mild slapstick with Schmidt's injury in the waterbed and doped-out behavior on Percodan. His speech at the wedding dinner teases us with expectations of a hostile outburst, but he ends by saying only the most conventional things.This movie will get more attention than `Pumpkin' or `The Good Girl' because of Jack Nicolson's presence: Solandz lite will inch closer to mainstream. And audiences will get a few big laughs -- Jack can create heavy irony with just a raised eyebrow -- but they will not get many. This is the saddest movie of the year, and the sadness isn't just from views of joyless marriage, helpless retirement, and death, but from a steady satiric vision of Middle America that is restrained yet deeply cruel. Anderson Payne has delivered a heavy wallop to the rebel in us. He himself, it is said, has moved away from Omaha and gone to live in Hollywood.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A rare cinema accomplishment. This is why I make films.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson gives the most Oscar-worthy performance of his career short of "Cuckoo's Nest." Understated, subtle, and remarkably HONEST. Jack is Schmidt. In rare form, Nicholson is truly vulnerable. The film is at once funny, poignant, and a true reflection of life in middle-America, or anywhere else for that matter. For once a script has been created, and a film produced that foregoes the fantasy that we as an audience are so accustomed to. "About Schmidt" is simply about Schmidt. And Nicholson's triumph is not in his portrayal of Warren Schmidt, but in how he inhabits that character in such a way as to hold up a mirror to the audience. I did not see Jack Nicholson playing the role of Warren Schmidt... I saw a real man reflecting on the experience of living; nothing more, nothing less. And that is beautiful.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Comb-overs and mullets, oh my!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson may have traded his wild-eyed gleam for a comb-over, but his talent for pouring himself into a role is as evident in this week's About Schmidt as it was 28 years ago in One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest.As Warren Schmidt, Nicholson is befuddled, to say the least, when, just days after his grudging retirement, his wife of many years drops dead on the kitchen floor. As if that weren't enough, Warren's only child (Hope Davis) has her heart set on marrying the best mullet and mustache combo middle America has to offer (played with aplomb by Dermot Mulroney).Alexander Payne and Jim Taylor wrote the dead-on screenplay, which should come as no surprise to those who've seen their previous collaborations, Citizen Ruth (1996) and Election (1999). Payne and Taylor are able to take the seemingly banal setting of Payne's native Omaha and reveal the lascivious and perhaps pitiful stories hidden among the cornfields. (Those who remember the line in Election referring to the level of moisture in a particular nether-region of Reese Witherspoon's body might be just as wide-eyed when a saucy, stark-naked Kathy Bates attempts to seduce Nicholson's Schmidt.)About Schmidt is by far the year's most original movie, (yes, more original than Spike Jonze's wacko Adaptation), due in no small part to uncanny performances by Bates and Nicholson-not to mention Payne's sympathetic direction and that phenomenal script (all of which are up for Golden Globes this year). It is even nominated for the grandest, most golden Globe of all, Best Picture-Drama, which begs the question, if the movie's that good, doesn't Mulroney's mustache deserve at least an honorable mention?</fullReview>

		<username>Ralph Michael Stein (riglltesobxs@mailinator.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Jack Nicholson Shines in "About Schmidt"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"About Schmidt" got into my mind and my gut slowly but steadily. Director Alexander Payne has given us a portrait of a man's late-in-life discovery of the anguish of loneliness and the possibility of overcoming sadnesses he never identified let alone confronted.Jack Nicholson, in a bravura performance worthy of another Oscar, is Warren Schmidt, an insurance executive in his mid-sixties who retires as the movie opens, his place taken by a young and stereotypically facile man boasting about the value of his Drake business degree.Schmidt has been married for four decades to his now overweight wife and for more than thirty years to his employer. An actuary by training, his role at his company was spectacularly unglamorous while internally essential to his employer's business interests.It's not a spoiler to tell, as all the reviews have, that Schmidt is suddenly widowed early in the movie. This loss comes at a time when the daughter he remembers adoringly as a little girl in pigtails is engaged to be married to a man about whom Warren is less than ecstatic.Nicholson invests his role by alternating a take-charge business persona that did much for him when in the insurance industry with the unwelcome intrusiveness of the intermeddling dad. Jeannie, his daughter, is well into her thirties. She knows what she wants. Hope Davis, a very good actress, plays a woman with an ordinary job and...a really ordinary life. Her fiance is a nice guy, not much more. She deals with shipping for an electronics outfit. He sells waterbeds. This isn't a power marriage about to happen.Driving a very expensive Winnebago, Warren takes off sightseeing, showing up in Denver in time for the nuptials. His host is the fiance's mom, portrayed with humor by the great Kathy Bates. (She should get a Best Supporting Actress Oscar for her role here.) She comes close to stealing some scenes from Nicholson.Nicholson's depth is astounding. He slowly realizes something is wrong with his life but he can't identify, at first, the issue. Torn between supporting his daughter's choice and giving unwanted advice, he's forced to reexamine his own life and Nicholson, overweight and slightly disheveled most of the time, does so with increasingly subtle but mounting ferocity.The theme of elderly loneliness, in this country and for those who seem to have all the material advantages, isn't new to film. But here the director and cast portray ordinary albeit in some ways wacky folks with whom we can empathize. At least I did.Is Nicholson a better man, a happier one, at the end of the film? That's a question he leaves us with.9/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A slow moving journey about loneliness and isolation.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"About Schmidt" is director Alexander Payne's third in a trilogy of films he's shot in his hometown, Omaha, Nebraska. "Citizen Ruth" and "Election" were the first two."Schmidt" is a study of the loneliness and isolation of a retired actuary named Warren Schmidt played brilliantly by Jack Nicholson. Schmidt's life has passed him by. His salvation is trying to reconnect with his alienated daughter after the death of his wife.The film's cinematography captures the grayness of Schmidt's life. We see the Midwest as a flat, rolling surface-a blank canvas for Schmidt's empty existence."Schmidt" is a regionalist masterpiece like the Coen brothers' "Fargo" and 'O Brother Where Art Thou." Payne's humor is a bit different, less dark. He's more tweaking and loving of his subject matter and his origins.Kathy Bates, Hope Davis, and Dermot Mulroney are part of the fantastic supporting cast. Even the hairstylist in this flick should see an Oscar nomination. Nicholson's combover and Mulroney's mullet alone are worth the price of admission.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>This is Mr. Payne's third attempt to make a watchable film. In "About Shmidt," he cobbles together a series of unused vignettes from "Election" and "Citizen Ruth" that were best left on the cutting room floor. The multi-talented although aging star of the film is clearly exasperated with the take-no-prisoners, bad-boy attitude of his baby-faced wunderkind director. When Payne wants to distract your attention from the painful truth on the actors' faces, which is often, he irritates you with a blaring soundtrack. When he wants you to see the eye-twinkling, unpretentious, home-cooked honesty of his Oklahoman kinfolk, trapped-in-their-ring-around-the-collar, middle american, ways, he cuts to parades, clowns, and orderly town meetings, not unlike those you might see on public access at 2:00 a.m. Contrasted with those not-so-subtle filmmaking techniques is Payne's judicious and plentiful use of in-your-face, shameless nudity. That alone almost made my experience worth the price of admission. Thank you for this film!</fullReview>

		<date>2004-11-26</date>
		<username>escapoholic (johnconley@aol.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Jack does dull!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(NOTE: used caps where I wanted to do italics--sorry if it's offputtting)ABOUT SCHMIDT is nothing if not a tragedy, and no less so for its farcical elements. Alexander Payne's style works when his characters' tragedies could maybe be ours if we were them, but thank God we're not. ELECTION works wonderfully that way. ABOUT SCHMIDT crashes because it doesn't work at all that way.After several shots of skyscrapers with slums at their feet, Arthur Schmidt (Jack) is introduced staring at a clock ticking towards 5PM in an office where we see nothing except for boxes and his desk and chair, looking like Randall McMurphy if he'd survived the lobotomy and gone on into a career as an actuary. Plainly the pooch is long since screwed in this man's life, done deal, let's move on.There's nowhere for the movie to go after this except for balls-to-the-wall farce, ELECTION style, which seems a possibility given that it's Jack Nicholson. Maybe there's a Randall McMurphy type back story, indeed seems like there'd have to be. After the lobotomy, though, do you really expect anything interesting to happen? You shouldn't and it doesn't.Next is the retirement party, where it looks like Schmidt didn't just miss the train, but the railroad long since went into bankruptcy and the station collapsed around his ears. Similar idea although a decidedly different feel from the office scene. Events have been passing this man by for a long long time. How did Schmidt/Jack get HERE? Subsequently, we see that Schmidt must have been in the midst of things at the office somewhere in the not-too-distant past. He visits his whippersnapper successor in his office and we see it's top-of-the-line in the office hierarchy. (By the way (NOT), how excruciatingly pathetic is it that Schmidt submits himself to the totally inevitable humiliation that everybody but him sees coming from this visit? Payne is demanding you get something out of this scene that was totally not there for me.) And the letters to Ndugu tell a story of a man really not much interested in anything outside the office but plainly a player in the office's confines. Schmidt's is a 1950's story glaringly out of place in the 21st century.From all this it seems we are to understand that Schmidt's life is unspeakably bleak and Schmidt himself has been rendered dull to the max by some kind of repression the essence (and possible resolution?) of which is the story of the rest of the movie. And by the way, he's from Omaha and one significant recollection he has is of an event (I've forgotten what) attended by folks all the way from Des Moines and Wichita. Don't think for one second that THAT isn't as significant as it gets.I frankly don't see how you can think there's a story here unless you buy the proposition the director seems determined to advance that you really can't distinguish between repressed and dull, at least amongst typical Midwestern folks. And much as you might want to try to read depth into this character from subtleties of Nicholson's performance and nuances in the screenplay, there's no denying that the major effort at work for Jack is to dull it down some way you never thought Jack Nicholson could.I won't deny he succeeds, but even if he does, so what? How Jack got that damned dull would be a story, given that it's Jack. What he does having gotten that way, though, isn't, even if it is Jack. I don't think you really want to see this performance unless you'd like to see how Michael Jordan will look on the hardwood after he's in the throes of acute arthritis.I won't deny also there are bits and pieces of a very good movie here. One I haven't seen mentioned is the scene with the Eau Claire RV folks--we can buy that these are real people because we don't have to see too much of them. The actors do yeoman like work. There's real pathos and humor in the letters to Ndugu. But as a plot device, the Ndugu angle is, well, a plot device--a plain acknowledgment in the screenplay that there would never be enough of a story in the day-to-day doings of this man to make a movie out of.I saw a review recently of SIDEWAYS complaining that Alexander Payne seems as a director as though his characters are beneath him. I didn't mind this or even see it in ELECTION, maybe because that movie makes a good argument for life as farce even while it's characters seem to be taking it seriously, which I can hope SIDEWAYS does as well. ABOUT SCHMIDT, though, is about something else entirely. Maybe Jack Nicholson can do dull, but Alexander Payne plainly can't do tragedy.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-02-11</date>
		<username>endo23</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>It's interesting to me...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>...that a lot of the user comments here equate "depressing" with "bad," as if great films are inherently uplifting (see Citizen Kane, Network, or The Godfather for irrefutable proof to the contrary). Films are under no obligation to make the viewer feel good, and this misconception is the reason so many modern Hollywood productions are transparent, maudlin wastes of time and money.For most of us, life doesn't consist of fending off the advances of airbrushed-beautiful women, or tossing magically-forged jewelry into the fires of Mt. Doom. And happy endings are the exception rather than the rule. Real life is a pretty tragic affair, even if the tragedies are minor compared to those of Hamlet or Oedipus. About Schmidt is a chronicle of just these sort of tragedies, and is clearly made with the belief that art should reflect life. The idea is that seeing real people on-screen (as opposed to, say, the ladies of Charlie's Angels) will encourage viewers to relate to the film on a deeper level and connect the characters' failings, foibles and triumphs with their own. In this, the film succeeds. The first time I saw it, a bunch of 30-something Hollywood industry folk gathered outside the screening room afterward and asked each other, "What are we doing with our lives?" If you live in LA, you already know that this kind of introspection is about as common as snow in mid-July.I'm not saying you have to love About Schmidt--taste is subjective, of course--but dismissing it as "bad" or a "waste of time" because it is a downer betrays a certain amount of naivete about the art of filmmaking. This film isn't trying to make you feel good--it's trying to make you question the trajectory of your life. If you can't stand feeling bad for the two hours this film runs, imagine Warren Schmidt's position when his wife dies and he finds himself utterly alone. That's the kind of depression you should fear; and that's the point.A close friend of mine called this film "sincere" and I think that's the word for it. There's nothing in About Schmidt that isn't honest and heartfelt, and that's a rarity these days. Cherish it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>william (willsgb)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>beautiful</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERS WITHIN, I THINKI was told this film was a comedy when we rented it out. The title also said to me 'camp stuck up stereotypical Hollywood comedy'. It didn't start well either. It seemed mundane and boring. There were no fast movements, no really major events. But i must say by the end i was actually emotionally involved. The ending really touched me, which is strange, because i tend not to get involved with films in that way. i honestly felt for jacks character warren. I also felt for his little friend in Africa, Ngudu! The comedy was tender and very REAL. Warren retires from his insurance company, and he feels inadequate because he was never the boss but the assistant manager i think. Anyway he's watching the TV, switching the channels looking for something interesting to fill the time - something im sure a lot of us can relate to, and another REAL touch of this film - when he spots a charity third world commercial, which gradually catches his attention. Anyway he gets a pack, which introduces him to 6 year-old Ngudu, who he'll be sponsoring for 22 dollars a month. It also asks to send a letter so warren writes his letter. This is the first real moment of comedy but its good. He pours his heart out about his life. He talks badly about his wife.He goes out to post the letter to Ngudu and comes back to find his wife has died. He has neglected her and it's another metaphor for depression and loss and waste. Anyway he writes again to Ngudu after the funeral. At the funeral his daughter comes over with her fiancé, this trailer trash bed salesman. Warren argues with daughter over marrying this man and the funeral. He's obviously at odds with her. She meanwhile is estranged from her father, angry with and yet loving of her father. The fiancé meanwhile proposes business after trying to console warren. Anyway once they've all gone, warren descends into a life of squalor, hilariously denied in his next letter to Ngudu. Another funny moment follows when he writes that he misses her. Moments later he finds a box of letters from her lover, his best friend. He goes maniacal for once in the film - he plays a timid tired man - and hits his best friend. Then he trashes all his wife's stuff, before embarking on a road trip, which he intends to find his soul and peace on. He tries to go to see his daughter early ? the wedding is on soon - but she rejects him. This prompts the soul searching.He goes to a camper van park where he meets a couple that like to meet random people. This is a metaphor for the desperate measures that people go to, to find friendship and love, i think. Anyway he kisses the woman after reading the wrong signals, and has to leave. This is he searching for affection from anyone who can give it. Then he meditates, and forgives his wife. Then he goes to attend the wedding. He meets his soon to be son in laws mother, who is a slob and open about her sexuality. The whole family is a disgrace, and he tries to persuade his daughter to not marry into it. She won't listen to him - they have been distanced. He attends the wedding and gives a forced speech at the cringe worthy reception. After leaving he contemplates his failure, and writes to Ngudu. He writes back - the hilarity of his writing to Ngudu - both the content of the letters, and the fact that he is writing in quite grown up language to a 6 year old boy in a third world country - is fully revealed. But the nurse looking after him at the nursery writes a heartfelt reply, with messages from Ngudu to him, who although indeed cannot write or read thinks about Warren and his kindness every day. Enclosed is a beautiful little picture drawn by the boy. Warren finally cries and its truly a heart wrenching moment.For me the films success comes in its realism - little touches like the daughter going to open the car door after arguing with warren, and having to ask her boyfriend to unlock it, his terrible hairstyles when waking up, his bored watching of the TV, his checking of the phone boxes money thing for change, and so forth. The message of this film is that it can relate to all depressed and tired people, this is a result of conformist lives, as the retirement party emphasizes. Basically, open your heart, don't waste time fighting or not being interested and/or involved in the lives of those you care about, because that's what's really important. He was open to Ngudu, and got the love and affection and interest he perhaps craved. He failed to stop his daughter marrying this man he hates, but realised the value of communication, love and life in his tears at the end, one hopes. The film is very beautiful, with slow and quiet classical music, great and heartfelt performances by every actor and actress, and SUBTLE and underlying humour. It's a real masterpiece, and probably one of Nicholson's best, in a distinguished career.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>When A Minor Action Makes the Difference of a Lifetime</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Warren R. Schmidt (Jack Nicholson, with an outstanding performance) is a typical middle class American and has just retired. He has worked for many years as an actuary in a great insurance company and now he is starting to adapt himself to live without one of the pillars of his life, his job. One day, while watching television, he decides to 'adopt' a six years old boy from Tanzania. Together with a check of US$ 22,00 per month, he is also supposed to write a letter to the kid. While writing these letters, he 'opens his heart', describing his life, his frustrations, his lost dreams, like he were in a psychologist. He is married for forty-two years with his wife and he has a daughter living in Denver, who will marry a very simple man pretty soon. He misses his daughter. A few days after his retirement, his wife dies, and Schmidt loses his second pillar, his family. Alone, his routine life ruins and Schmidt gets his Adventurer's trailer and starts traveling through some specific places in America, like the house were he spent the first years of his life, or some museum that he had never had the chance to visit. His trip ends in Denver, two days before the wedding of his daughter. He stays for a couple of days with her fiancé's family. Meanwhile, he keeps writing to his foster kid, and finds many hidden facts about himself, in a parallel interior journey to his lifestyle and behavior, questioning how common and plane has been his life, without making any difference. A wonderful end will show him that sometimes a minor action indeed makes the difference. This movie is very beautiful. Slow, but dense and touching, with a marvelous performance of Jack Nicholson, who supports the whole plot. I have imagined the situation of his character, losing in an advanced age and almost at the same time, everything that has supported his life. I really felt very sorrow for him. There is also a great message regarding the importance of First World countries supporting children of Third World countries with love, not hate. The supporting cast is composed by great actors and actresses, therefore this film is a pleasure for the viewer. Alternating funny situations, like a black comedy, and drama, it may be not indicated for all audiences, specially those in advanced ages. But viewers who likes sensible and touching movies, with a great direction and cast, and a stunning performance of Jack Nicholson, will not be disappointed. My vote is eight.Title (Brazil): "As Confiss?es de Schmidt" ("The Confessions of Schmidt")</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I was prepared to enjoy this film because it had gotten good reviews and I had seen some funny clips featuring two of Hollywood's biggest stars, Jack Nicholson and Kathy Bates. But it was a disappointing movie.It's a dark comedy about a recently retired man who's just lost his wife and goes out on an adventure to try and reconnect with his daughter and find meaning in life. The setup is good. Nicholson is introduced as Warren Schmidt, an emotionally detached husband and father. he character is so out of line with his public persona we expect Nicholson to play with it, but the laughs only come in spurts.The thing that bothers me about this film is the overwhelmingly negative view it has of people in general. We see things through Schmidt's eyes, and it's not a pretty world out there. His wife is a nag, his daughter barely talks to him, the person who replaced him at his job is a dolt, he finds out his best friend had an affair with his wife, his prospective in-laws are slobs, everywhere he goes on the road he meets dull people. The world view you're presented with here is that everyone is stupid and superficial. The situations Schmidt goes through are humorous, don't get me wrong, but there's an ugliness to the laughter, like the people behind the film are laughing at us, not with us. Everyone is dull-witted and visually unappealing, which tells you something about the writer's view of the world. Frankly, it just left me with a bad taste in my mouth.Jack Nicholson was nominated for an Oscar as best actor, but I didn't think his performance was all that great. He's got the character down pat, but I thought he did it much better in "As Good As It Gets." At least in that film his character undergoes change. Here, he looks like someone on prozac most of the time.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Hard to sit through the whole thing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Occasionally, a movie comes along where one or more of the main characters is flawed and so strikingly formed by the acting and writing, that it is difficult to watch these people. Jack Nicholson as Schmidt is such a character. To endure this man's post retirement life and the unsubstantial persona developed through his work and family life is a challenge. On one hand, you have to applaud the work of director Payne and Nicholson to paint a vivid portrait of this woeful character, but be prepared to squirm a little when situations arise you know he isn't prepared to gracefully manage. The strength of this ability is that Schmidt has phenomenally true characteristics imbued in people we know and meet everyday. It is too scary to contemplate. And that is why, as a movie watcher, we want to look away at times because he is an embarrassment, someone we don't want to be in whole or part. If you've seen Scorsese's movie "The King of Comedy", De Niro's Pupkin was flawed as well, different flaws than Schmidt, but still making you sweat by watching him because they are so truly lifelike. What I don't buy in this movie is the whole "Meaning of Life" theme and Schmidt's resolution in the end to this theme. Depending on your view of life, the ending will support whatever philosophy you may have in this regard. Frankly, it can be done more simply and definitively with a glass of water filled up halfway. By the way, you don't see many movies based out of Omaha, its worth seeing if you've ever lived in or around there. One of the other reviewers took exception to casting Midwesterners as collectively troubled and narrow-minded, similar to those who felt "Field of Dreams" made Iowans look like country bumpkins. Hey, it's the movie business, folks. This may be in part due to Nicholson doing a bang-up job of portraying Schmidt, but the rest of the cast, especially the more screwball parts, don't ring true (except for Schmidt's wife - I wanted to hug her).</fullReview>

		<username>tfrizzell</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Being Jack Nicholson.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson (Oscar-nominated) has put in his time and retirement from his insurance agency in Omaha, Nebraska is imminent. All should be peaches and cream now, right? Well, not necessarily. Nicholson is scared to death of his daughter's (Hope Davis) upcoming marriage to waterbed salesman Dermot Mulroney in Colorado and is obviously unhappy in his life as he wonders what he has done to make a difference in the world. Shortly after his wife's (played by June Squibb) sudden death, Nicholson begins to examine his life through his own eyes and decides that a road trip in his new Winnebago is just the thing he needs. What follows is a "Harry and Tonto"-styled film without the Tonto. As his daughter's marriage looms, Nicholson goes to Denver to stay with Mulroney's out-there family (led by priceless Oscar-nominee Kathy Bates and ex-husband Howard Hesseman). There the film continues its simultaneous comedic and dramatic assault on its audience. Nicholson's feelings are made clear by personal writings to an unseen foster child in Tanzania that he sponsors through one of those television commercials. "About Schmidt" is a strange dime-turner of a film that gets real dramatic and then switches quickly to comedy and vice versa. The film is heart-breaking and heart-warming at the same time. Nicholson makes the whole project work with yet another ungodly role that just adds to his greatness. Growing old is never easy to do and the range that Nicholson (who almost always plays younger than he really is) brings to the part is revolutionary. A joy, and admittedly sometimes a trial to get through, "About Schmidt" is overall an enjoyable cinematic experience that works because of extremely likeable characters and a truly original and life-affirming tale. 4.5 out of 5 stars.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A breath of fresh air in modern cinema.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>About Schmidt tells next to no story, yet kept me riveted to the end. The film is a portrait of the main character, Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson), drawn through a series of events in his life just after retirement. Nicholson plays an utterly believable, very boring person. Nicholson is surrounded by a strong cast: from the not-too-beautiful daughter to the daughter's father-in-law's 'Yoko Ono'ish second wife. Such a film needs slight eccentricities to offset the dreariness of Nicholson's character. Nobody is utterly perfect, yet nobody is too repulsive. The film is full of small jokes and quite sarcasm to alleviate the mood, such as a large interstate bridge to commemorate the progress of the original pilgrims, contentedly appreciated by our man Schmidt.Note, if your idea of a good film is a hundred minutes of fighting, or non-stop slapstick, this is not the film for you. Those with Scary Movie or Matrix 2 &amp; 3 posters slapped throughout their rooms may find it a bit of a yawn. But don't despair: there's a chance to see a fat woman's breasts for you to giggle at.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>One of the Best Endings in Hollywood History</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is gem of a little film, set in Nebraska, and starring a sterling Jack Nicholson as a recently retired VP who looks back at his life, trying to understand its meaning and his place. Despite some mis-labels that it's a comedy, it's truly a drama with some ample doses of humor. Refreshingly, Jack plays his age (about 66), with a wife who is utterly real, a great performance by June Squibb. All the characters are realistically portrayed with superb support by Hope Davis as their only daughter, Dermot Mulroney as her fiance, Kathy Bates as his mother, Howard Hesseman as his father, many more. The film has lots of surprises and an ingenious plot device of Nicholson telling his thoughts by writing letters to a needy African boy called Ndugu. My favorite scenes book-end the film: in the beginning, we see Nicholson sitting in his office watching his clock tick to 5:00 P.M. on his very last day. The film covers repression, loneliness, aging, conformity, beautifully expressed by Nicholson. It all leads to a completely unexpected, and most gratifying conclusion. If you want aliens, explosions, car wrecks, look elsewhere. But don't miss one of 2002's best.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-03-26</date>
		<username>trapperjohn1210</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One Of Jack's Best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>At 66 years old, Jack Nicholoson hasn't lost his touch as one of the best actors in American cinema. The three-time winner of the Oscar for Best Actor always manages to pick projects that capture the audience's hearts and imaginations. And with his latest project, "About Schmidt," his streak continues. Nicholson plays Warren Schmidt, a recently retired life insurance actuary searching for meaning in his life. He realizes very quickly that his life has hit rock bottom. His wife of 42 years has just died, he finds out afterward that his wife and his best friend had an affair 25 years ago, his former job has been given to an eager young hotshot and finds that any of his own input is useless and unwelcome, and his daughter is about to marry Randal (Dermot Mulroney) - a mullet-sporting mattress salesman that has "loser" written all over him. But, he finds an output for his frustrations by sponsoring a young Tanzanian boy named "Ndugu," to whom Schmidt sends several letters about his life, or the life he thinks he's living. Fed up with the way things are going, he sets out on a road trip in his Winnebago for a much needed break from reality in the weeks before his daughter's wedding, to which he is constantly trying to stop. At the end of his journeys, he arrives at the home of Randal's mother, Roberta (Kathy Bates). He then finds, to his displeasure, that his future son-in-law's entire family brings a whole new meaning to the term "dysfunctional family." Roberta does try hard to make Warren feel as comfortable and at home as possible, but that's impossible. In the end, through the wedding, Warren makes his journey home, still not finding the meaning to his life he hoped he'd find and has realized that he spent the last 60 years of his life with blinders on. But, hope does arrivefor him in the form of a letter from the nun who takes care of and eductaes young Ndugu. "About Schmidt" is the warmest comedy of the year, and no doubt one of the best. The entire film encompasses a feeling of reality that could depict almost anyone's life. The film has plenty of laughs, but does rely on lowbrow humor or Hollywood gimmicks to get them. The laughs are plenty and honest. It wouldn't surprise me at all if Nicholson took home a fourth Oscar for his portrayal of Schmidt. Nicholson gives off a performance that is funny, touching, and saddening at the same time. Bates also is comic perfection in her role of the control-freak, foul-mouthed mother-in-law. But, the most over-looked performance of all is Dermot Mulroney's portrayal of Randal. He's a typical loser, but he's a charming loser. Randal brings in laughs, not only with his rediculous hair, but with his overall being, and one of the worst wedding vows in history in which we learn just how many seconds and minutes there are in a day. "About Schmidt" is my pick for the best comedy of the year. On a scale of one to ten, definitely "About Schmidt" desevres a ten. Filmmakers should look to this film to learn what a great American film can be.</fullReview>
	</review>

Warren's life is boring and dull nothing is happening in his life and he wonders is this all that there is left for him. This really provides the heart for the film as we hear what Warren writes in his letters to the young boy and it serves as narration for what is happening in Warren's life. After his wife dies rather suddenly, Warren decides to get away and takes a trip in the new RV they had bought. 		<fullReview>This movie is deeply touching. Although I am only 26 years old, and the movie is about retirement, it helped me put things in perspective. How can more than 30 years at a company be blown away by contributing USD22 per month to become a foster father of an African child?All in all, the movie is deeply moving. 		<date>2003-02-13</date>
		<username>Eric Perreault (Redemption)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Tragicomedy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There seems to be a fundamental dislike of films that might have some note of "depressing" subject matter for American viewers. I can never understand this. I think this speaks volumes of a nation so hung up on seratonin reuptake inhibitors to "wash our blues away". I for one find a certain honesty in films which are not afraid to poke around those dark corners of the human soul, because in reality, life seldom wraps up with a happy ending by the time the credits roll. That said, my thoughts: (Spoilers) About Schmidt comes across as a very funny and yet terribly tragic comment on life after retirement. Warren Schmidt is the restrained everyman we encounter daily, often unnoticed, and perhaps so close to ourselves that the film hits a bit too close to home. Schmidt took the path we have often been taught to accept...He went to college, got a good safe job, had a family. He seems well liked and respected by his cohorts and lives a comfortable middle class life. And yet upon his retirement he is faced by the haunting realization that in fourty years of gainful employment, he has contributed virtually nothing to society. Upon the death of his wife, he embarks on a winnebago tour of self discovery, pouring out his thoughts in letters to a six year old adopted Tanzanian boy named Ndugu (who doubtless cannot understand a word Schmidt writes,) precisely because he has no one else to talk to. This trip culminates with the marriage of his daughter to a waterbed salesman deemed by Schmidt to be sub-par for his princess, but he deals with her choice with characteristic midwestern restraint. Feeling totally lost and alone in his life, he returns to an empty house a self proclaimed failure, only to find that his great life question has been, in some small way, answered in a child's drawing. And if you are human, you should be crying at the end of this film. Nicholson makes Schmidt the restrained master of repressed emotion. I am reminded of literary characters like "Alfred Lambert" In Jonathan Franzen's "The Corrections" or Chris Ware's epic cartoon novel "Jimmy Corrigan, the Smartest Kid on Earth". Here we see what can happen when the banality of corporate offices and meaningless jobs reduce life to an automatic routine allowing the important things to pass by unnoticed. If only Schmidt had been reading "Organization Man" when he was in college, or listened to Malvina Reynolds sing about lives made out of ticky-tacky all looking the same, he might have arrived at 66 in better shape.Without a doubt, one of the best movies of 2002. You won't be hit over the head by anything (this movie is so restrained it hurts) and I might compare it to "The Strait Story" for its slow and deliberate pace and quiet revelation at its conclusion. If you don't have an attention span, skip it because I don't want to have to read another response about this movie being boring. And yes, it is depressing, but hey, that's life. About Schmidt doesn't take the easy way out. You want bubble gum? Watch Britney Spears. You want great cinema, see this.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>ilovedolby</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Excellent Performances Make One Of 2002s Finest Films!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jack Nicholson, who is no stranger to greatness in motion pictures, gives another superb performance as Warren Schmidt in Alexander Payne's new film, About Schmidt. Jack Nicholson has performed in such wonderful films as As Good As It Gets, The Shinning, A Few Good Men, and who could forget his hilarious/psychotic roll as the Joker in 1989s Batman. However, About Schmidt is a far cry from Batman. In fact, this film is somewhat comparable to American Beauty in that its main characters are too men who have been completely disillusioned by life and it's experiences. But About Schmidt's Warren Schmidt, is not nearly as disillusioned as American Beauty's Lester Burnham. I would say that Warren is simply tired of living a life that is a letdown and is flat-out boring. But the real problem is that Warren has absolutely no idea what life is really all about to begin with. Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson) is a 66 year-old retiree and shortly after his retirement as vice-president from a large insurance agency in Omaha, Nebraska, his wife (June Squibb) dies. Having no one around to keep the house clean, or make him three square meals a day, Warren slips into a state of absolute childhood; he only watches television, doesn't clean up, and eats TV dinners by the dozen. The only things that really seem to matter to Warren are his daughter, Jeannie (Hope Davis) and the letters that he writes to an orphan in African village, named Ndugu. Realizing that there is nothing left for him at home after his wife's death, Warren hits the road in a huge Recreational Vehicle (RV) that he and his wife bought prior to her death. He heads out to Kansas for his daughter's wedding to a man that Warren despises, played by Dermot Mulroney. When he arrives, he meets his future in-laws played by Kathy Bates and Howard Hesseman. Kathy Bates plays the groom's mother, Roberta, who is a little more than unorthodox, from lying naked in a hot tub with Warren, to revealing the secrets about her sex-drive. In the end, it's up to Warren to accept the challenges and tribulations of life and make the best of things, or else, what else wallow in a state of pity for the rest of his existence. This film is quite amusing at times, especially when we see Warren struggle through his problems. Probably the funniest parts of the film occur when Warren meets Roberta, played by Kathy Bates. Bates steals the show with her hilarious take on the mother in a dysfunctional family. And of course, the reports of nudity of Kathy Bates are true. Although very brief, we don't see anything that is all too revealing at all. Nonetheless, she gives a great performance, as does Nicholson as the disillusioned, Warren Schmidt. Nicholson, in fact, just won the Golden Globe for Best Actor for his performance in About Schmidt. When I first heard this last week, I was a bit apprehensive. I was hoping for Daniel Day-Lewis's performance in Gangs of New York. However, after seeing About Schmidt, it is clear that Nicholson did deserve the award. If I had been a judge for the awards, it would have been difficult for me to choose which man for the award. But one can't complain about Nicholson. His performance is top notch. About Schmidt is very funny and not at all what I was expecting. I highly recommend About Schmidt for anyone looking for a change of pace from the normal comedy genre films currently playing, such as Just Married. Although it starts out a bit slow, don't let that deter your enjoyment from this excellent motion picture. ****</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>What about Schmidt?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So, Warren Schmidt is a boring, cheap bastard who's having a life crisis, and... well, that's about it. I guess the film is supposed to be about his literal/figurative journey towards a better life, but as far as I could see he didn't really get anywhere.The film is deliberately slowly paced, but that doesn't have to mean that it's boring as well (which it, sadly, is for the most part). With a running time just shy of two hours, it's not a good thing when it takes 45 minutes for something to happen. The film could easily have been trimmed down quite a bit, and been all the better for it.Normally, I don't like Nicholson's (over)acting, so it's a bit of a paradox that I think that the film picks up pace only when Nicholson does something other than staring out into space with a sullen look on his face. I loved his voice-over bits, though, maybe he should do an audiobook or something.There are some good things in the film (the score, Dermot Mulroney, and a couple of funny scenes), but on the whole, I would have expected more from director Payne, whose previous film Election is a real treat. As for About Schmidt, I shrug my shoulders, say "What about Schmidt?", and give it a (barely) passing grade. [4/10]</fullReview>
