		<date>2003-01-22</date>
		<username>tedg (tedg@filmsfolded.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Which Film?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers herein.Brando invented the modern acting technique of giving you two films: the one in which the characters exist, and another in which the actors exist. Nicholson has developed that into something rather clever. The basic job is the same, to inhabit a character and create a truth so sharp you think you knew it all along. But the second layer for Nicholson -- at least in these later films, say after `Chinatown' -- is to reflect another film. So the question when watching one of his newer projects is: while we see him in this film, what film does he see himself in?David Lynch made `The Straight Story' with `Easy Rider' in mind, and I think the film Nicholson created here as his shadow film is `Easy,` through `Straight.' That scene by the river is Lynchian.But the tone the director had in mind was very close to Christopher Guest, where every being is terminally pathetic in some way. `Best in Show,' in fact has a scene with Guest traveling along a road which reminded me very much of the visual narrative that Payne spins.Peter Greenaway made an extraordinary film (`Death in the Seine') which illustrated notes from a Paris coroner on corpses found in the river. These are exquisitely presented views of completely anonymous bodies, who have had worse than a worthless life, they have no life at all. More, these deaths occurred during the brief use of an odd calendar after the French revolution -- when the calendar was restored, these deaths were virtually erased. By bringing these eclipsed lives to film, he makes them more real than they ever were in life. I think that is what Jack is sometimes about these days; taking a worthless man, in a worthless environment (city, profession, family, even Rush Limbaugh!) and making him bigger through archetyping by force. It is something like the attention given to the plastic bag in `American Beauty,' but made personal by lifting our own ordinariness a smidge.Every other character is background noise.Ted's Evaluation -- 3 of 4: Worth watching.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-01-02</date>
		<username>Emily (elh2018@columbia.edu)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>About what?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Perhaps if About Schmidt were about something other than nothingness, I would have found myself actually caring about Schmidt as I watched this movie. But as it was, the film really didn't make me think or feel much of anything. Sure, Warren Schmidt's character is pitiable - it's hard not to sympathize with someone whose life is devoid of any real passion or human connection, and Jack Nicholson, of course, brings Warren as much to life as is possible.Warren has just retired from his position as vice president at an insurance agency, only to find himself wondering who the old woman who shares his bed is, and how his daughter Jeanie can really plan to marry the ponytail-wielding, pyramid scheme-vending man she calls her fiance. So, when his wife is out of the picture, Warren embarks on a meandering road trip that will eventually land him in Denver to try to stop his daughter's wedding.While Warren's adventures, if one can call them such, are portrayed with a satiric edge, the tone of the movie is quite maudlin, not at all what one expects from director Alexander Payne, whose brilliant Election managed to pick its characters apart yet still garner viewers' sympathy for them. About Schmidt, on the other hand, is unevenly satiric. Some characters, like Jeanie's fiance and his mother, are utter caricatures who serve mainly to remind both Warren and the viewer that he can't care about anything surrounding him. When he finally meets someone who notices his sadness, someone he thinks he can care about - in an R.V. park, no less - he is reminded, with the pain and awkwardness we expect from Payne, that life has room for very little human understanding.The only real emotional connection he is able to forge is with a six-year-old Tanzanian boy, Ndugu, whom Warren adopts through a charity. Letters to Ndugu become Warren's outlet, and the movie is filled with voiceovers narrating Warren's adventures and emotional state. Perhaps it sounds as if this plot line is not meant to be taken seriously, as if it keeps with Payne's signature satiric style. If only such a presumption were true. One does laugh upon hearing Warren beginning yet another letter to his illiterate young friend and revealing absurd details about his life, such as the fact that his wife used to make him urinate sitting down so as not to get the toilet seat wet. But Payne seems to be just as earnest about Ndugu and all that he represents as Warren himself does. The way that Ndugu ends up being the one person whose life Warren has significantly touched and therefore the one person who significantly touches Warren is meant to be viewed sentimentally, not cynically.It seemed to me, after watching About Schmidt, that Payne wants us to feel the pain Warren's emptiness has caused him, and to find hope and significance in the `new life' we see him beginning over the course of the film. The problem is that we aren't sure who to care about. The lives of the characters presented to us are laughably pathetic, but frighteningly resonant. While watching this movie, we aren't sure whether to laugh or to cry, to love or to hate, because the film itself, like Warren Schmidt, hasn't quite figured out what it's about.</fullReview>
	</review>
</movie>
